=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Roll a fistful of dice, which are then used to allocate action cubes for future actions (choose fewer cubes for earlier actions or more cubes for later actions).  Use these cubes to do the following: 1) move your ship (this takes a long time initially but is worth the investment as shipping goods is a major way to score points), 2) build ability cards (you need to get these built anyways as they score you minus points if they're not built over the course of the game), 3) move ahead on the turn order track (yes it's always good to go first), 4) take over a city quarter (this gets you the requisite goods to ship, and also scores some points at game end), and 5) discard cubes for money (if you have the appropriate cards built).  Money is exclusively used to purchase points, and because it is scarce and the points you can purchase are significant, you'll want a way to get some income.

I was initially quite turned off by this game, as it seemed like a real grind, and a luckfest.  Further plays have shown that there is some real depth and replayability (and it plays great as a 2er).  The dice can be frustrating at times.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Macao is a game where if you do not have a plan, than you are planning to fail. Also, luck helps. This is definitely one of my favorite Felds. Not because the currency abreviation is "PP" either. While hilarious, APs, PPs, etc are probably the only horrible part of this game. The art is also not why I like this game so much because well... it isn't anything to write home about. No this is one of my favorites because it is just a great game. There are a lot of moving parts in Macao but they all come together in a synergistic way. These parts include dice rolls to create a pool of colored cubes used for actions, two boards, cards, and a rondal that drives a color coded cube action allowance system to keep the game in motion. The two boards consist of one that is primarily about pick up and deliver and the other about area control and set collection. You must progress on one in order for the other to matter. The card flops and drafting every round force you to make choices as to what cards and associated powers you THINK you will be able to purchase down the line using specific combinations of cubes. These cubes were placed by you on a rondal that determines what cubes are going to be avilable to you on what round. The rondal drives the game, the cubes fuel the rondal, you steer the game using the rondal. If you fail to plan and guide your specific actions and choices accordingly, then things start going wrong. Horribly horribly wrong. Cards add up and suddenly you are taking negative points for cards you drafted that you cannot purchase using the proper combination of cubes. Ships stop being able to move to locations or are not loaded with the proper cargo to get you any points because you didn't have the right cubes to purchase the right districts and carge which means you lose influence on key districts and thus end game points. Finally, Stefan Feld comes by hosts a comedy central roast in your honor where all of your friends take turns pointing out your shortcomings (ok not the last part). Truly Macao is magnificent and reminicent of other Felds that punish you for bad choices or just bad luck like Notre Dam. Highly recommended by me. Especially if S&M is your kind of kink.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.12
Abstract: 
An innovative euro game by Stefan Feld. Each player has a Windrose - a septemgonal board numbered from 1 to 6 plus one arrow symbol - to collect colored cubes. Players get contract cards which they have to fulfill by spending colored cubes. Fulfilled contracts grant special abilities. There is also a central board which is split into a shipping area (race game) and a district area (set collection / area control).

Round overview:
a) reveal contract cards 
b) determine exchange value for prestige (victory) points
c) each player takes one contract (in player order)
d) colored dice are rolled to determine where and how much colored cubes can be placed at the Windrose 
e) each player choose 2 dice and places the matching cubes at his Windrose
f) all Windroses are turned one spot to the right
g) players can use cubes at the arrow symbol spot of the Windrose for different actions

Actions (mostly activated by spending cubes):
a) activate (fulfill) contracts by spending the required cubes
b) move ship to deliver collected trade goods
c) move forward on the track for player order (wall track)
d) claim a district in the city and collect a good tile
e) exchange prestige points for money (once per round, only money needed)
f) use special abilities from activated contracts

Pros:
+ Innovative and challenging Windrose mechanic
+ Multi-used dice which determine the type (color) and quantity of resources, and the round on which they can be used
+ A big number of different contracts with special abilities (only half of them are used each game)
+ Lot of special abilities and combinations (engine-building, set-collection, different strategies)
+ Set collection for good tiles which is also used for area control in the city. On top of that, players have to keep an eye on the shipping routes to ship the collected good in a efficient way
+ There a few joker/wild tiles in the city which can be collected instead of good tiles. The joker tiles grant either a free cube or 3 money which adds more flexibility especially when activating the difficult contracts
+ Competition for being first player (wall track). Having first dips on the newly revealed cards is a big advantage
+ Penalty if you have too many contracts creates tension
+ Variable exchange rate for prestige points: a bit push your luck
+ Interesting mix between long-time planning and make the best of the current situation
+ Plays well with all player counts but differently (less competitive with 2 players since there is more space and tiles)
+ Helpful player aid integrated on player boards (but formatting and small font make it a bit hard to scan)

Neutrals:
# Indirect player interaction: be first to collect and trade goods, first choice on contracts, area-control for districts
# Some luck and randomness: dice rolls, revealed contract cards, and good tiles distribution
# Sometimes a player might be multiple times lucky or unlucky regarding rolled dice or revealed cards (luck of draw)
# There are over 120 contract cards and players get only a few which makes it very difficult or even unlikely to collect the right combination of cards (being first player helps)
# ... despite all luck and randomness: the game is all about mitigating luck and randomness by planning ahead and making sure to keep one's options open
# Can be unforgiving if not planning ahead smartly (especially on the first game)
# No hidden information (still enough randomness to prevent analysis paralysis)
# Very strategical in terms of picking the right contract cards or dice
# Sometimes tactical to get a trade good before someone else does or to avoid negative points
# Some rounds feel less productive if a player has only a few cubes (but there are also some super big turns)
# Moving ships to the first harbor feels very slowly (come on, get there in time!!)
# There are a lot of components (300 cubes!) but it does not feel fiddly
# May have some down-times (especially on higher player counts): each player does all actions at once (not turn by turn)
# Scoring may happen during the round and at the end of the game - scoring points happens not as often as in other games by Stefan Feld

Cons:
- Theme and artwork is okay, but dry and not very strong
- Challenging to get into the system on the first game
- Leftover money is useless at the end of the game (counts no prestige points)
- Subtle wording for how often a contract card can be used. Should have been clearly highlighted on the cards
- Rules are okay but icons on cards and goods are not properly introduced (pictures would help), moreover, the limited usage of cards and actions is not clearly explained

Thoughts:
I love the pure, unique design of Macao. It has many intertwined mechanics but still does not feel overwhelming like other games with similar complexity and crunchiness. Macao, like Bruges, is all about mitigating luck and creating an engine by clever planning. However, not all players will like the Windrose mechanic which is not that intuitive kind of a puzzle.

Similar games: 
* Bruges (like Macao all about luck-mitigation, more tactical, overall lighter game, easier to teach and play)

See also:
* FAQ / clarfication of cards: https://boardgamegeek.com/filepage/104427/macao-faq?error=captcha
* Player aid: https://boardgamegeek.com/filepage/108704/macao-player-aid?error=captcha
* Rules summary: https://boardgamegeek.com/filepage/59683/macao-rules-summary-and-player-aid

Player: 2-4 (best: 3-4) | Time: 50-100m

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.73
From what I had read of Macao, I was expecting to be profoundly disappointed with it, as I was with the only two other Stefan Feld games I have played (Rum&Pirates and Notre Dame). Macao has turned out to be an unbelievably pleasant surprise for me!
I am SHOCKED (SHOCKED, I say!) to hear people describe Macao as a tactical game! Certainly, an element of tactical thinking is required, but in reality, this is the Mother of All Long Term Strategic Planning Games! And that is due to the unique die roll-compass thingy. As far as long term strategy goes, I don't think I've played any other game that has such a plethora of options to be puzzled with. And such planning is not a dry exercise at all, due to the modicum of luck in the game that generates a deliciously agonizing uncertainty for players. I suspect that anyone who plays Macao as a tactical game would, indeed, find it to be dry and luck driven; but such illusions will fade away if you play while looking at the BIG strategic picture, and not focus merely on the round at hand. Luck driven it ain't, by ANY stretch of the imagination!
Nevertheless, there are a few problems with the game. The final stretch of the game is lacking in the delightful tension of the first two thirds of Macao, since the players have much fewer action points to work with by that point. Secondly, the only player interaction in the game lies in keeping an eye on what the other guy is up to; that's not a critical fault, but it's there. Thirdly, those who delight in analysis paralysis will be in Heaven with Macao: therefore, consider yourself warned!
Fourthly and finally, there is the question of theme. I am SHOCKED (SHOCKED, I say!) to hear people say that Macao is lacking in theme! Why, there's theme pasted all over the place in Macao! Theme that (sadly) has next to nothing to do with the game mechanics. Normally, a lack of theme can ruin a game for me, but not Macao. The game system is just too brilliant for me to be deterred by such petty things as theme!
Macao is beautiful! Brains a'smokin'!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8

It's not very deep, sure. I think that's what prevents me from playing it. But I have fun when I play it!

RATING: 7.1
This is an excellent game, and probably Feld's best that I've played (so far), which includes the likes of Notre Dame and In the Year of the Dragon.  There are some very interesting, innovative mechanics, and these are coupled with well-balanced game play that affords multiple paths to victory.  What's more, there are 96 unique Building/Person cards that give the game seemingly endless replay value. I will say that the rule book needs a lot of improvement--it might be the worst I've ever dealt with in terms of grammatical mistakes (thankfully not in terms of rule ambiguities, which are much more important).  It even includes this corny admonishment at the end regarding how players shouldn't try to manipulate rules to their advantage (very cheesy).

Update after further play:
I do not personally feel that I am one who typically suffers from AP (I do not, for example, suffer from it with respect to many games that are known for it--e.g. Tikal and Le Havre).  However, Macao is a notable exception.  There is just so much to consider in every facet of the game, and the diverse array of cards, while adding almost infinite replayability, only exacerbate the AP problem.  While it is true that many of Feld's games contain ample intricacies and things to consider (or else you'll get "punished" severely), it seems especially palpable with Macao.  For this reason, I'm downgrading Macao, placing it behind Notre Dame and In the Year of the Dragon.  A great game, but not as good as these two because of the AP issue...

RATING: 8
A really good game, with a very pleasing and fun combination of interesting and creative mechanics. The designer (Stefan Feld) has managed to make the rolling of dice, and the "gamer's desire to plan ahead on turns," fun in a very mellow and interesting way. 

Other nice touches include...lots of nicely colored cubes to accumulate and spend (without having to worry about what they represent...interesting), and a pleasing game board to look at.

Although this game uses materials we are all familiar with cubes, cards, dice and resources, it manages to feel, through the magic of capable game design, original and refreshing. Admittedly, confusing card terminology and card effects do present some challenges for first time Macao gamers, but all in all, Macao feels like the ideal game to give a friend and/or family that likes euros.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Another game which "uses dice in an interesting way".

Here the major mechanic is that the dice selected give a person a respective number of action cubes (kind of similar to action points) and that these action cubes become available to a person according to the value of the die he gets.  For example, choose a '6' value die to get 6 action cubes which will become available in 6 turns (5 rounds), choose a '1' value die to get 1 action cube to become available within that 1 turn (the current round), etc.

This mechanic creates a number of interesting dynamics.  Chief among them being how you weigh between getting some action cubes sooner or a lot of action cubes later.  These cubes then allow for a number of different options, giving players the ability to pursue different yet equally rewarding strategies.  Overall the mechanics are very intuitive and mesh well with one another.

However while I find the mechanics in Macao to be refreshing and interesting, I find the components to be very lackluster.  The tiles and cards are all thin (feels thinner than Catan tiles) and the tiles are cut awkwardly (such as the gold coins being circular in picture but not really cut in a way to make them feel like coins).

But despite these component issues, Macao is a another great example of how dice can be used and plays relatively quick for the amount of depth it presents.

RATING: 8
This is a solid game that features 2 elements that I enjoy.  First there is an area control / connection element in the warehouses that are claimed.  Second is the multiple combination effects that can be combined for effects.  This starts with the windmill and cube acquisitions and continues straight through to the scoring.  There are many elements to this game and many great things about it.  The replay factor is very high with the very limited (versus supplied) cards that can come into play during a game.  That said, that is also one of the draw backs.  Since there are so many possible cards, it is very difficult to plan for one to come up - and that means the outcome can swing based on who happens to be in a position to seize an opportunity.  All in all, its one of the strongest Alea titles in a while.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Macao is another clever, unusual, enticing game from the genius of Stefan Feld. Its core is built around the "wind rose", an heptagon used to allocate action points cubes through a clever dice-based mechanic. This mechanic, together with the hundred cards, has some push-your-luck flavor that's particularly tasty. And the presence of these two factors (dice plus cards) means that no two games will play similar...
There's obviously some flaws as well: the theme is absolutely pasted on, the production is not top-notch (thin cardboard chits, small cards unreadable from distance), and an end game that can be somewhat anti-climatic.
But, in the end, Macao is a game that exhudes genius, and it's a very worthy addition to a euro-lover collection!

RATING: 8
Normally I hate being at the mercy of random die rolls, but Feld managed to win me over with some clever mechanics.  You can mostly plan for the future by setting aside Action Cubes for later use.  Obviously the dice have a large impact on your game, but I do enjoy coming up with alternative plans to unfavorable rolls.

There are 3 major reasons to fight for turn order: Cards, City Markers, and delivering Wares.  It is possible to stay behind and win, but I prefer paying the AC to stay in the lead.  Others have commented that the theme is pasted on.  Personally, I feel like I am collecting wares and sailing a ship.  Scales very well.

RATING: 4
Many novel and clever mechanisms (ASPIRING BOARDGAME DESIGNER REAPPROPRIATION ALERT!) with little to recommend it as a gamer's game.

Luck arrives in spades, less so with the colored dice than with the card draft. Turn order is hugely important to getting  good choice of cards, true. Worse, most cards reward strategic commitment without providing a way to mitigate the luck that decides whether or not that commitment pays off.

If the game was just about cards and dice, it could be a lightweight game of risk management. Instead, an entire pickup-and-deliver game is bolted on along with two separate spatial puzzles.

On top of ALL OF THAT is a separate source of victory points, randomized each turn and rewarding players who happen to have saved the exact right amount of money.

Blech.

RATING: 7
[i]Posted: 4/5/11, Rated: 7[/i]

After a few questionable entries in their [family=58]Big Box series[/family], [company=9]alea[/company] has enjoyed a return to their former glory, due in large part to the design efforts of [person=4958]Stefan Feld[/person].  [i]Macao[/i] utilizes dice in a novel way to allocate future resources.  Combine that with the now ubiquitous deck of special power cards, and you have an interesting game.

I have a nagging suspicion that there might be a general solution to [i]Macao[/i], such as going for 5's and 6's, and then selecting your cards to match the colors you've already collected.  Then again, if everyone does the same thing, then only a few cards will be successfully activated, and everyone else will start taking penalties.  [i]Macao[/i] was pleasant enough, and I do look forward to seeing if it is hiding more depth.

RATING: 8.3
A Feld design that rewards the experienced player who is familiar with the (types of) cards in play and how best to leverage their Activity Cube (AC) resources efficiently to build, deliver goods, generate coinage (GC), and maximize Prestige Points (PP).  Each round players choose AC based on a shared dice roll and place them around their Wind Rose based on the die result.  The higher the number the more cubes of that color, but they are not available until later rounds.  Be careful of the penalties for having no AC in a given round or too many inactivated cards.

RATING: 10
I really enjoyed this one.  Like some of Feld's other games, you have to manage something so you don't take negative points.  This one you have to manage your cards and getting action cubes for each turn.  Easier said than done.  Well, if you focus on it, it's not an issue.  But it's easy enough to go astray.  Lots of different scoring that you need to contend with.  I don't think you can let something go and still win.  I also enjoyed the mechanic of getting action cubes AND how the cards worked.  It added pressure and choices.  You can long term plan in this one.  Also, you can set yourself up for the big turn, as well.  Overall, it was fun, tense and close.  I played this with two people.

I liked with four just as much.  My new comment:

It's what you can make and plan for out of randomness.

I think that sums it up for me.

April '10 - quickly becoming one of my two or three favorite Alea games.  Who am I kidding, this ranks right behind PR for me in Alea games and one of my favorite games at the moment. 

November 2011 - A 10.  I have now played 19 games of this, mostly 2 player.  With playing so many games, I have a great feel on what cards are in the deck and how the cards work together.  I try to let the early cards I draw dictate my plan.  I have discovered that the four cube single color cards are not difficult to get down...you just have to plan for it down the road.  A big key is to grab the joker card for the cube of any color.  This way you can just about get any four color cube card down in a reasonable amount of time and cash in the bennies.  Both my friend and I leave shipping more towards the end of the game and wall position is key.  You do not want to get stuck in last for the whole game.  Stay cose so when you decide you need to pick first, you can.  Just a ton of stuff to consider.  Also, with two, we blaze through a game in about 45 minutes or so.  The second 10 I have bestowed on an Alea game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
In The Year of the Dragon surprised me.  Stefan Feld hadn't struck me as someone that could put out such a superlative game.  Sadly, Macao sees Feld back to his old ways.  In fact, this strikes me as his worst game by a fairly large margin.

First, Feld needs to expand his scope a bit.  He basically starts with an economic engine game, adds some punishment and a gimmick to constrain actions.  Macao is no different. It's just an amalgamation of mechanics you have seen before with a dice/cube wheel (the aforementioned gimmick).

I actually like the gimmick, the trade-off of many cubes later, a few now is a sound one.  However, this game in no way takes advantage of it.  Long term planning is severely shortchanged by the roll of dice and which cards come up.  Potentially you can look at all the city quarters and the office cards to formulate a plan, but that is a large amount of information that I don't want to digest.

A huge problem here is the pacing and the constraints.  The first 4 rounds are very short and completely worthless.  You have no cubes.  The game opens up for about 6 rounds before you run out of cubes near the end of the game.  It's really demoralizing to have to play the game for so long before you can do anything.  

You get penalized if you skip a turn so no storing up for a big turn and you can't carry cubes between turns.  This doesn't work for the many cards that require multiple colors.  Some of them require 3 (or even 4) colors which is nigh impossible to get and are not exceptionally powerful for the cost.  In fact, the best strategy appears to go for cheap cards because their greatest benefit is 3 points which you get no matter the cost.  A surprising number of cards don't even seem worth building without this point bonus.  A much better design would have more interesting cards that people want to activate rather than compelling them through penalties.

Connecting city quarters seems unnecessarily frustrating as you need essentially the same cube color in increasing amounts to form a chain.  But you can buy only one quarter a round so you have to make sure to iterate that color and do it with a large number.  However, this mechanic does take the most advantage of the dice/cube wheel, which is I guess something.

Thematically, Macao is a mess.  You get cards, buy stuff in some kind of connection game in the quarters and then move a ship around to deliver stuff.  Oh and their are gold coins that do nothing but buy points at a ratio that changes every round.  It felt like Feld was trying to cram too many things into the game at once.  What does the dice wheel even represent?

Agricola and In the Year of the Dragon can feel punishing, but it's ultimately rewarding to play and overcome their tribulations.  Macao tells you to run that way and then turns around and hamstrings you.  You are constantly prodded by the game to do something while it hampers your attempts at following its orders with a healthy dose of randomness.  Just not a whole lot of fun.

I certainly hope Alea lets Feld go work on his own.  I don't know how it turned into his personal label.

RATING: 6
After 1 play I found it to be an enjoyable set collection and economic game with a refreshingly innovative dice mechanic.  However, after 2 plays, as much as I enjoy the mechanics and the plethora of options, this game is too long and too dependent on dice rolls.  If you pick a card that takes 4 red cubes and you never roll higher than 3 red during the entire game or if the colors you need simply will not match up at the right time points based on the die rolls, it can be a very painful experience.  Also, there are simply too many unique cards (~100) in the game and you only use half of them with the full complement of players so this considerably slows down the card drafting part of the game (compared to games like San Juan that have repeat cards, which lends to speedier decisions).  Your decisions are mostly involve guessing and hoping that you can obtain the right cubes at the right time points.  It feels like it needs something to mitigate some of the dice and card luck -- maybe allowing players to save one cube between rounds or allowing the placement of cubes one turn before or after the naturally rolled round for a cost of 1 gold and a penalty of 1 cube from the set.

MORE NOTES:  The "envoy" card is a bit of a game breaker since it is so powerful (allowing you to get a cube of your choice for purchasing points which is something you want to do anyway).

UPDATE 2014: My least favorite (by far) major Stephan Feld game.  Too much of the game hinges on luck and guessing.

OTHERS SAY:  A conundrum... a game about planning ahead, but it is centered around dice rolls and card randomness.  First seems like kinder, gentler In the Year of the Dragon or ___, but this facade hides a nasty engine that ruthlessly hands out negative points to unwary gamers.  Novel/clever use of dice.  Roll a handful of multi-colored dice, and then everyone selects 3 to keep. You then get action points in the color of the die equal to its face value, but don't receive them until X turns later where X equals the face value of the die.

RATING: 6
Typical Alea game. From the artwork to the mechanics everything screams Alea in my opinion.

As the available action cubes for several futire rounds are known in advance it is possible (and probably necessary) to plan relatively far ahead.

There are definitely several roads to victory (money engine --> buy VP every round, heavy shipping, cards), but in order to really play strategically I think you have to know most of the cards, which is a turn-down for me.

Also, later turns (when lots of cards have been activated already) seem to produce a lot of downtime.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I love the Alea Big Box series very much, and Macao is a fine addition to a lineage of great games.  Like Loyang, this uses a limited draft, and like Loyang, you will find more chance elements in this one than your average big boxer.  I have no problem with this, but it will turn some off.  There are many nice little tensions and trade-offs in this game, and so far, there seems to be no sure path to victory.  Certainly there are many paths to defeat, and it may be frustrating to some to find that what seems to be a game about getting a gold/VP engine going is actually a game about damage control and buying the least bad of the cards on offer.  The main rule for this one is: get those cards off your tableau!  Aside from that, plan on accomplishing only about half of what you'd like, as you'll only be able to buy VPs every other turn at best.  Like Loyang, this is a bell-shaped game - fast turns at the beginning and the end, with long ones in the middle.  I like the rotating wheel/action accumulation mechanic, there is real tension in deciding to take many cubes down the road or a few in the coming turns.  Of Feld's contributions to the BB line, I think I like this one the best - even given the chance elements, I find it the most challenging and brutal (although ItYotD is probably more brutal with multiple players).  Nice and solid.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I held off on getting this for a long time, but my newfound love for all-things-Feld made me seek it out. I was not disappointed. Despite the dice and the tableau-building, the game actually resembles an economic game on a small scale. You can try to accomplish short-term goals with a poorer payoff or plan huge moves far in the future, though you'll get punished heavily in the intervening turns if you plan too far in advance. Finding the right balance is the key to success. 

Normally I hate games that require lots of calculation, but it's really a fun exercise to try to look at your cards and the spaces on the board, and then figure out how many cubes of which colors you'll be able to get in X turns. 

My biggest complaint has to do with the cards: too many of them give the same ability, often at vastly varying costs, so it is sometimes annoying to have to choose among cards that do what your completed cards already do. I also wish there were more variety to the cards (i.e. an expansion!). It's a neat little design, though, one I didn't expect to enjoy.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Another solid release from alea and Stefan Feld; they've sort of jumped on the Agricola bandwagon with lots of different cards with lots of special powers that you need to play. Identifiable as a Stefan Feld, it's got that pressure of penalties if you don't get certain thing done. (FWIW, some players would like the game better if simply got 3VP for every card you played instead of a 3VP penalty for unplayed cards).

I felt that Notre Dame was a little light theme-wise, but Macao really feels very theme-deficient to me.

Most Stefan Feld games have taken a while to grow on me. Notre Dame especially grew on my as I played it more, especially as I limited it to smaller number of players. Macao will probably fall into this category as well, although for different reasons. Macao feels mechanically clever and pretty clean, but thematically clunky. As I play more the theme may feel better, allowing the rating to rise.

Because of the theme deficiency and the complexity I suspect the audience for Macao is pretty narrow, mostly the serious hobbyist gamers amongst us, moreso even than most alea games.

But for now anyway, I'm enjoying it and look forward to playing it some more.

RATING: 8.5

Nonetheless, this game is good, enjoyable exercise for the ol' noggin.

It doesn't matter much, but the color scheme was dull as can be.  Also, the text on the cards is tiny.

RATING: 6
The big problem with this game is that the game plays you. The game asks you to formulate a long term strategy by forcing you to take cards that have building requirements.  Then, the game randomizes what combination of building requirements you get in each turn - which can be eternally frustrating or amazing if your lucky enough.  I love games where the dice randomizes the play area and everyone has to adapt - and this masquerades as a game like that - but it can totally screw you while helping your neighbor at the same time, so its not really like that.  It seemed to me that the game was just Yspahan with an additional layer of abstraction (the cubes) and more 'hoping' you get the die roll you need.  On the plus side, the card powers are fun and the cube/dice mechanism is new and refreshing.  Hopefully with another play I will feel differently.

RATING: 8
Has quite a bit of luck, but a most can be mitigated (concentrating on specific cube colors, knowing the deck, working with offices etc). The game has enough meat that the luck does not bother me, quite the opposite. There exist 4 basic strategies (buying VPs, goods area control, efficient goods delivering, card comboing) but one must stay tactical and keep one's options open. 

Interaction is usually subtle, but can get quite fierce towards the end of the game, when one can see the good he needs being snatched under his feet. Turn order is very important, especially at 3-4 players. The game requires forward planning ala Trajan or Luna, and stands happily shoulder to shoulder with both these games.

Overall, Macao is a well oiled system that still keeps entertaining after quite some years, and does not suffer from the point explosion or unnecessary intertwining complexity that has plagued the last Feld games. A vintage Feld, and a really smooth one. Keeper.

RATING: 6

Rating down to a 7.
------------
Feld is on a roll. That's four for four with me on his Alea big box games. This is hands-down better than Notre Dame. I think most people will prefer this over Rum & Pirates, although personally I have a soft spot for that game. It's hard to compare to In The Year of the Dragon, only because that game is significantly simpler. Macao has a bunch of cards that do stuff that you need to acquire, and what I learned in my single playing so far is that you can just get whatever and hope it works out. You need a plan. Those with a plan will be rewarded and those without will be punished. The game feels "hard" like ITYotD, in that at first glance the game taunts you with lots of stuff to do, but it's actually really hard to do stuff. But the core mechanic is realy neat. The use of dice is in a shared way that doesn't really screw anyone individually, but the cool thing is that you get action cubes which you allocate to specific future turns. The more in the future you allocate them, the more you get (in general), so it's almost like an action point savings plan. At the end of the day you're doing some fairly mundate stuff (collecting resource chits, selling them at foreign ports), but how you do it feels pretty fresh.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Macao was played first by me at a long since gone Spiel (edition 2009 to be exact) where my partner and I had commandeered a small table deep within the Ravensburger enclosure. We could finally play a game in quiet there (as that episode was unusually busy and crowded); we only had to tell off one other person. We found the game fairly easy going, with what appeared to be a cute dice mechanism, but my partner complained about having a disfunctional card engine due to bad overlap between her cards. I contributed it in part to being new to the game, and in another to being a sore loser. We didn't purchase a copy, though, knowing that a Dutch edition would arrive soon, and that we'd need another attempt to make up our minds for sure. That opportunity came a few months later, but it more or less turned me around on Macao completely. I was stuck in exactly the same position as my partner this time, and couldn't really make the system work in my favour. The capriciousness of the dice, which have you wildly guessing at what you need when, soon became a thing to loathe with a passion instead of recognising it as a novelty in game mechanism design. Cards turning up when you least need them, but being forced to pick them up just the same, was very annoying. The final straw was the habit of the game to punish you for things you don't really have any genuine control over. I have since avoided the game for years on end until I was made to play the game one final time. And lo and behold, the game still behaved as treacherously as in my memory. In the end I probably didn't do too bad, though.

My main beef with Macao is that the game rewards long-term planning while offering you very few tools to do exactly that. You have no say in how the dice turn out as they do, but the combination between colour and number of pips is crucial. Only two cards are known per turn... but in order to have first pick you must invest really heavily in the turn order scheme and brave the swells of the Sea of Player Chaos. Or face the consequences, which is a pattern Feld revisted and elaborated (and then some!) in the even more clamping-down-on-the-players'-genitalia title AquaSphere. There is no real way to bypass the system: you either undergo its ministrations or you don't play the game at all. I chose the latter option, and hope that it will be a very, [i]very[/i] long time ere I experience them again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Interesting twist on the action point allowance genre.  What makes the game unique is you select action cubes tied to dice rolled in those colors, and then assign them around a 7-sized dial at their appropriate space (5 cubes goes next to the 5).  Then you rotate the dial once space, and use the cubes formerly at the 1 spot for your turn.  This leads to a need to think several turns ahead at what you may want to do later.

Definitely challenging to figure out how to do all you want to do, but this game moves fast and is easy to understand once you get moving.  This is a tight little game from Stefan Feld that was instantly satisfactory and made me want to play it again.

RATING: 8
The first Feld I acquired. Took me several games to get my head around planning for the Windrose. Macao definitely grew on me after several plays. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
The game proper is great: fantastic combination of short-term tactical optimization with long-term planning in the face of uncertainty. It really takes a full game or two to get a sense for "how difficult is [card X] going to be to get out?", and longer than that to get a good handle on valuing many of the cards in the person/building deck... but can be played decently (and be a lot of fun) on the first go-round. The better you get at the game, the more valuable turn order becomes, because the better you are at recognizing what to draft.

The production/translation could have been much better done, though; Rio Grande kind of fell down on this one: there are multiple ambiguous (or unintuitive-to-English-speakers) translations, a card titled "Office" that is not an Office card but a Building card, acronyms ("AC" and "GC") instead of clear icons, no pictures on the Warehouse cards to show what type of goods they operate on, and more. It makes me very sad - the game's so good, it's a shame that it's marred by these factors, particularly as they make it harder to teach to new players.

[Edit: I'm now beginning to feel like initiative order is a little *too* important - that the disparity in drafting order with experienced players is huge, mostly, though the ability to claim/ship first can also be quite relevant. This should in theory self-correct, because the opportunity cost of spending cubes on initiative in early game can be quite high... but I'm not sure I like the overall dynamic. Still love the core planning puzzle, though.]

RATING: 7
Components: Attractive board. Lots of tiny cubes. The cards are a bit plain, and could do a better job of distinguishing some of the types of information (e.g. w/ iconography instead of words for the office cards), but are otherwise functional.

Experience: In this game, the size of the components doesn't matter. The shipping lanes, occupying two-thirds of the board, occupies only a very small part of the game and final score. The quarters, filling out the rest of the board, matter a bit more, but not by much. Most of my attention is on my dice rondel and on my player board. I've never so needed to look explicitly into the future during a game, trying to build up the right combinations of cube colors on each upcoming turn. There are many different routes to take, for different costs, leading to distinct strategies among the players. The toughest decision each round is in how to allocate the dice.

First Played: 2010-08-22
Last Played: 2013-01-25
Eagerness to Play Again: Soon

2 players: 2

RATING: 6
Pros:
- Medium weight euro
- Dice mechanism balanced since it is a pool of options everyone chooses from
- Cube/action management is unique

Cons:
- Long term strategy planning is restricted to card draw and draft
- Strategies seem limited as scoring is primarily made up of the same things: acquiring a district, delivering goods, avoiding negative VP by activating cards. So replayability is a concern despite card variability.

Initial Impressions:
Each turn is a puzzle that's presented to you in terms of which cards to pick, which action cubes to choose from the dice, and how to use those cubes to best further your path to getting the most points. Managing this type of challenge is exactly what I like about euro-style board games.

The plethora of cards available means that no two games should be very similar. Some small randomization of the board setup and each turn has a variable amount of points to buy.

The theme isn't too strong because I don't feel like a trader but that hardly matters.

RATING: 9
There's a 7-sided "cube wheel" each players gets in this game. Each round, you rotate the wheel one space and get to use the cubes on that space.

Also each round, you get to load cubes onto a couple spaces... but the trick is that to get larger numbers of cubes, you have to place them on further away spaces--meaning you won't get to use them for many rounds.

Beyond that, the game has a shipping/delivery element, a battle for turn order, the ability to acquire different types of buildings and people (all with different powers), and an unusual variable exchange rate victory point purchasing option.

Lots of good stuff here.

RATING: 7
Has mechanics that I love.  Dice that aren't dice, power cards to buy, sets to pick up and deliver.  But the challenge of getting your plan to work just isn't in your hands.  In San Juan and RftG, if you want a card, you can work towards getting it.  Here, you may just never roll the right purple number, and never see a purple generating card.  The hand size of 5 seems like a kludge to cover the fact that it can just be impossible to do anything.  I don't want to not do anything.  

I love me my dice, but the resource model should be changed to something deterministic.  Like cube color levels grow, and taking a color resets the count or gives you a penalty to taking that color again or something.  Then increase land costs.  So then you'd balance taking short piles to get multi colored cards out and waiting for big piles to gather.  Or something.  Something other than "Oh god, just roll purple near red one gorram time."

RATING: 8
Players are trying to acquire prestige in the city of Macao.  Solid little "engine" game where players are forced to take a card each turn, which they can activate, or ignore for an end of game penalty.  Different, and slightly weird, system where cubes are allocated out onto an action wheel, which is then ticked over to give your your cubes for the coming round.  Cubes are spent on driving your ship, acquiring quarters of the city, activating your reserved cards, and so on.  Engaging game, thematically disconnected, but does hook you in.  The early turns fly by, but it bogs down as the cubes pile up, and the final four turns seem to take an age.  Would love it if it was 30 minutes faster, other than that, excellent ideas and a good game.

RATING: 7
THE DEALBREAKERS: The pacing is off; the most interesting part of the game ends before the game does.  By the time you finally get your engine together, it is just not that satisfying to turn the crank on it.  Too much dependency on what dice rolls and cards come up for a calculating cube-pusher.  

I generally appreciate this game and the way all the parts fit together.  At first, I liked the challenge of trying to put together the right engine to allow the purchase of PP each turn.  If you can but PP on just one turn when your opponents can't, you'll have a big advantage.  I also like the trade-off between a couple cubes now or a bunch of cubes later.

However, I'm finding this less interesting with repeated plays.  You need to deal with multiple levels of randomness, so you can't count on either the dice or the cards you'd like to see come up.

I think my biggest issue with the game is with its pacing.  You can't do a whole lot at the beginning. Then, you do a bunch of stuff towards the middle and finally get your engine in play, but then in the last third of the game you are awash in cubes and pretty much just mopping up.  That last third takes the longest to play out because you need to dink all your cards and deal with all your cubes.  Consequently, the game loses some momentum at the endgame.

What is up with the thin cardboard?  The tokens in this game and Notre Dame are barely acceptable.  Come on guys, this is the Alea Big Box series!  Step it up!

So to sum up: I found the game quite interesting at first, mainly due to the dice mechanism, but the game lost its luster for me with more plays.

RATING: 6
There is something about Stefan Feld games that feel very similar.   I think it is the penalization, maybe.  Notre Dame has that, In The Year Of The Dragon has it, and this game has it too.   In other ways, it's like a combination of mini-games:  build the biggest cluster on a grid, make deliveries on a map, select abilities/characters from a pool, jockey for turn order on a track.   It's all stuff we've seen before.   Except...  for the dice mechanic, which amounts to an action point system for the current turn and future turns.  Without this, the game wouldn't work.   Good stuff, I'd like to play it some more.

RATING: 8

Lots of choices

The mechanics of the game in general are interesting and allow forward planning.
Your moves can affect other players

Cons
The setting is cool, but the rules aren't always consistent with it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[1 play] I can't say there was any great novelty to this game.  Au contraire, it was yet another Euro with a bunch of cubes, set back in the day, with a trade theme, named after a place.  Stop me if you’ve heard this one before!  And yet I found it pretty agreeable.  

The first thing that struck me about this game was that there was a big heap of green cubes, but they told me I couldn’t be green.  What?!?!  Turns out that the most common colors (actually, green, purple, red, blue, black, gray) have been used for resources, and the player colors are the leftover obscure colors (orange, brown, white, and I’m not sure what else because we had a 3p game).  This seemed like an interesting reversal of the usual approach.  Not sure if there might be a difference in which way is more colorblind-friendly.  

Macao does actually have some nifty and interesting mechanics (and it’s definitely another for the "clever nontraditional uses of dice in Euros" category).  Foremost among these is the wind rose.  We actually began the game with a discussion of whether this should or should not rightly be called a rondel.  When someone says "rondel" to me, I think of the rondel as used in Imperial or Finca, where the slices of the wheel are different actions and you (attempt to) move around to the one you want.  In a way, the wind rose in Macao is sort of the reverse: the wheel moves steadily, and you stack the slices with the resources you want to put in different positions to enable different actions when you get there.  I thought this was really cool.  

One thing I really like about this game is that, although many of the things you can do, and probably the ones you’re most intent on trying to accomplish, require specific combinations of resources, there are other actions (the turn-order wall, sailing your ship) that can be done with ANY resources you have.  It’s very groovy that even if things don’t work out just as you’d like, still nothing goes to waste.  

I struggled in the first half of this game, but made a sweeping comeback to win by a substantial margin.  The Bank (free gold every turn) and the Diplomat (allows you to do the tribute action--buying victory points--twice) were a powerful combination.  The Diplomat was strong only because I had gold to spend and the tribute ratios were favorable, but I think the Bank would always be great to have.  It also helped that I took 6s a lot in the early rounds, and built up a bunch of cubes for later (though not quite enough!).  I think the cards that give you extra ship movement would also be tremendously worth having.  

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: N/A
One of Feld's very best.  A bit more [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/777972/macao-or-burgundy]unforgiving than Castles of Burgundy[/url].

Macao evidences true innovation in its game design, and is a veritable triumph of mechanics over theme. The innovative use of dice in the wind rose action cube mechanic, as well as the variety and replayability in the 120+ cards are two shining stars of game design. Given the labyrinth of multiple strategies and depth of gameplay, players will find themselves desperately trying to manage whatever random elements are present, and finding combinations and strategies to exploit in their quest for victory. Experienced gamers who enjoy the challenge of the classic cube-pushing euro and are prepared to immerse themselves in this masterpiece, will readily appreciate the genius evident in Stefan Feld's Macao, and find themselves richly rewarded with return visits.

Full review: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/986599

RATING: 5
What the hell? Stefan Feld is one sado-masochistic dude. You are severely limited in your ability to plan but are penalized for when plans don't come through precisely. Holy crap. I felt like I was spinning plates on my toes standing on the back of an elephant on a tricycle riding over a pit of lava and God forbid I should drop anything. I'm OK with being penalized for lack of planning, but I don't like being penalized for planning well but then being totally screwed through no fault of my own. The game commits one of my most detested gaming crimes by catching players in AP as they plan for the risks they wish to take but then having all their planning undone by the dice anyway. Good thing we spent all that time planning, eh? A game for masochists.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
The more I play, the better it gets.  Great use of dice to employ an action point allowance system.  Players must manage the benefit of getting little cubes early vs. more later. Gaining money is also important in order to gain VPs through the tribute track.

Many interweaving things to consider, and player interaction is mostly focused on who gets what first - whether it be shipping an item, taking a card, or placing an ownership token in the city quarters.  Takes about 90-120 minutes to play.  There is some slight downtime with reading the text on the cards, but after more games are played the cards become more familiar and 2nd nature.

This is an excellent game with relatively little to complain about.  Everything works well.  While the gameplay is smooth, the struggle to do what you would like is anything but!  There is a noticeable pace to the game.  Towards the end there is a "critical mass" buildup with the combinations of cards players accumulate.  Some brainburning is involved to decipher the best timing of actions, what resources to get, and the best order to use the activated cards.

Stefan Feld is becoming one of the "sure bets" in boardgame design, and is one I will continue to look out for in the future.  Grade: A+

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Rules Complexity: :star::star::star::halfstar::nostar:
Strategic Depth: :star::star::star::star::nostar:
Luck Factor :star::star::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Negotiation Factor: :nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Amount of Downtime: :star::halfstar::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Fiddliness: :star::star::halfstar::nostar::nostar:
Replayability: :star::star::star::star::star:
Component Quality: :star::star::star::star::nostar:
Player Interaction: :star::halfstar::nostar::nostar::nostar:
"Take That" Conflict: :nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Theme Integration: :halfstar::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar:

If you're into specific action planning up to 6 turns in advance with a generous sprinkling of luck, then this is THE game for you!  It's a fairly heavy game that seems to take a little longer to play than your average Alea big box game.  It features the best dice mechanic I've seen yet, and I enjoyed this as much as I was hoping!.  There is a lot going on in this one - some may even say too much.  The 120 cards all with unique text and characteristics reminds me of the barrier that was broken with Agricola's multitude of cards.  In this game there aren't as many total cards as Agricola, but many more will come into play within each game, which means a fair bit of reading must be done by everyone.  As the players activate more and more cards, it's almost impossible to keep track of what players have which special abilities that influence what decisions which way.  This would probably make my list of "Top 5 games not to play with someone who suffers from heavy analysis-paralysis", but I think this game can be played reasonably well just going with instinct.  Some may gripe about the fact that the cardboard components aren't as sturdy as Carcassonne tiles, but I found the quality to be acceptable.  There is some significant luck in this one with the Tribute track and the way cards become available for acquisition, but much of the point of the game is about being ready for opportunities when they come.  I'm not sure that I've seen this much luck (some would even say chaos) combined with this depth of strategy and fiddliness in one game before.  Some may also complain about the lack of player interaction.  Basically the interaction boils down to claiming certain city spaces before others, claiming cards before others, and shipping specific goods before others, and there is nothing direct.  While it may sound like there are a number of potential negatives, I think this game is actually a LOT of fun.  I prefer playing with as few people as possible as it could get pretty long with 4, but it is time well spent.  It gives you lots of options, sets you up with specific dreams of what you can do, gives you significant tension and then rewards as you make your dreams a reality.  Don't let a casual gamer anywhere near this, but it's a very solid gamer's game if you don't mind the luck.

RATING: 7
Macao is an odd beast of a game. On one hand it's a forward-planning cube conversion game with a strong strategic component, on the other it's a long string of random card flips and a dice bonanza.

That doesn't just sound like a weird combination, it also plays like one. Getting the proper resources at the right time is of paramount importance, and both the cards and the dice can throw one hell of a spanner in the works.

Maybe that means you need to approach the game from a different angle. With a bit of careful planning the most egregious punishments can usually be avoided. In typical Feld fashion that makes the game one of averting disaster rather than constructively building anything. If you look at it like this the game seems rather more manageable. It does seem, however, that if for some strange reason the dice do decide to align benevolently there can be huge windfalls for the lucky player. On the whole I'm still kind of undecided on whether the influence of the dice exceeds the leverage of the players themselves but until I find out Macao provides a very unusual challenge.

RATING: 6
Macao is an OOP game I was excited to play but fell short of my expectations. Some of my issues with the game:

-The wind rose, the card play, and the mini-games on the board all felt disjointed in a cobbled together kind of way. 
-The card draw is too random and swingy. 
-Going first is potentially too powerful due to the ways cards range in their extreme activation prices.
-Money is insanely tight.
-Buying points, building connected shipping houses, shipping goods, and planning out cubes for future actions ultimately made for boring game decisions.
-Too much set up and faffing about for what it amounts too.

These issues make the game feel much older than 2009. I think, by today's standards, this game would have gotten reworked quite a bit. A couple things that stand out in my mind that would be simple fixes:

-Give players income of 1 to 2 gold per round. Or,  give them one of those starting office cards that allows them to exchange 1 cube for 1 gold. Or, even better yet, allow players at any time to sell a certain amount of cubes for gold. 
-Separate cards into three "eras", something like what you see in Glen More, London, or a dozen other games. First era cards would help you get your engine up and running, second era cards would allow you to develop your strategy through specific abilities, and third era cards would give you really cool abilities but you'd only be able to possibly use them  in the last four rounds of the game.

Another Feld where I can honestly say: I'd rather play Rialto.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Macao is, in many respects, just your standard "build a little personal economic engine and then churn victory points" type of Euro.  We've seen it all before a million times.  Well, almost.  Macao's one fascinating innovation is the numbered dial thingy that the rules call the "wind rose" but that I'm going to call the "Cube Chronometer."

Each turn someone rolls six standard dice, each a different color.  The players can each choose to use two of these six dice, with no restrictions regarding the other players' choices (you always have full choice among the six).  If I choose "green 1" and "black 5" I get one green cube and five black cubes.  But not immediately.  The cubes are placed beside the corresponding number of the Cube Chronometer, then the Cube Chronometer is rotated one position clockwise.  The arrow now points to the cubes that I have available this turn.  So the single green cube is available to me this turn, but I'll have to wait four more turns before using the five black cubes.

The cards you're trying to "build" require cubes in different numbers and color combinations, but - and this is very important - you're not allowed to store cubes between turns.  Use them or lose them.  So the game is a challenge of trying to get the right numbers of cubes in the right colors at the right times.  The dice don't always cooperate, and so you're constantly having to evaluate the odds and create contingency plans and rejigger your strategies.  The game requires a certain amount of planning, but the "perfect planner" type of player is likely to be frustrated here.  Also, Macao is mostly a parallel solitaire type of game.  There is some very important interaction, but most of the time you're playing in your own sandbox.

And that's what's keeping Macao from earning a higher rating from me. As the game progresses, I care less and less about what my opponents are doing in their sandboxes. They're messing about during their turn, doing a bunch of little micro-actions from their various card abilities. These little actions are hard for other players to keep track of, since they're written on tiny cards sitting in front of someone else. And honestly, the other players' little bags of tricks rarely affect me or matter to me. I usually just kind of tune them out and trust that they're doing it right. Playing with two or three players instead of four helps. So does playing with people who can execute their actions quickly.

I do also have some small complaints about a few of the cards*, but overall I do enjoy Macao and the strange type of inexact planning it requires.  For me, this is among Stefan Feld's best games, probably #3 behind Trajan and The Castles of Burgundy and ahead of In the Year of the Dragon.

* specifically, I think the cards that have more powerful effects in combination with another specifically-named card are a bad idea.  The deck is huge, so there's a high probability that the desired card won't even enter the game.  And there's no way to preview the cards in order to know whether the partner card will become available. Also many of the cards are imbalanced and tend toward degeneracy, but I view that as more of a feature than a bug; it increases the importance of the turn order "wall" and adds drama to the game by rewarding ambitious strategies.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.5
Players rotate their ship's wheels each round, then use the cubes available to them in that round to perform various actions: activating cards selected in that round or earlier rounds, buying city quarters and collecting the goods located there, moving that player's ship around Europe to deliver those goods, acquiring gold coins, taking special actions with card previously activated, and advancing on a turn order track.

Players score points by delivering goods, paying gold coins, using the powers on their cards, and building in Macao. Whoever has the most points at the end of twelve rounds wins.

RATING: 8
This game adds a new and exciting mechanism which could be called "future action points management".  Players need to allocate action points for upcoming turns (at most 6 turns in advance) and they need to deal with 5 different types of action points.  In addition to that, in each turn you face a number of critical decisions: do I need few actions NOW or do I need many action points later?  And which types of action points do I need?  

Furthermore it has a very nice mechanism of "action cards" which are bought for action points and which can be combined to form powerful combos.

All in all an excellent game (not for the casual gamer, though).  Highly recommended!

Trade notice: German edition

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
An interesting game from the maker of Notre Dame and In the Year of the Dragon.  There are definitely some similarities between all three of these games -- you have many types of commodities or categories which you are trying to balance, and you have to plan ahead a few turns to make sure that you don't get penalized due to running out of some commodity due to the inevitable upcoming events.

There is constant tension between the desire to acquire small numbers of goods cubes early (in order to activate cards from your tableau, to get the benefit of those cards as well as to avoid getting penalty points for taking a 6th card) vs. the desire to get large numbers of cubes in a much later turn (in order to activate more cards at once or to move your ship as far as possible, as well as to avoid getting penalty points for having no cubes in a turn).  Throw in the race to the front of the wall (to get the best cards that either give the best special power or cost the least to activate), the race to get a nice set of wares and a contiguous set of warehouses, and the race to get to the wares destinations first... there is a lot to keep track of!

I don't immediately see a winning strategy, especially since the card draws and dice rolls are so random.  I suppose that one should remain flexible, so that you can deal with the randomness.  I think that in the early game, getting to the front of the wall is pretty important, so as to get first pick of the early cards, whereas it isn't so important at the end of the game, when you might have more cubes to pay for more advanced cards, and when the cards can't be used for many turns (and hence are not as valuable).

It's important to note that on the last turn, you'll have to pick a new card, and if you don't have the cubes to activate it, you'll get a -3 penalty at the end of the game.  So it's important strategically to have a variety of cubes available in the last turn.

There are some rules (on the cards) which are unclear:
- Host+Pub = 3 GC per turn *combined*, not 6 GC/turn
- Baronesses: 3 PP for having one, 8 PP for having two, 15 PP for having all three
- Crane/Healer/Archive: *each* Building/Person/Office card (respectively) costs 1 less AC to activate, not just one activation per turn

========

At times, I really dislike this game... it's because of the ridiculous dice rolls, which can be very unbalanced.  You could say that everybody is facing the same dice, but everybody has selected different dice in past, and has different future requirements on the cards they selected (and those selections were made based on a guess of what was going to come).  So bad dice rolls can cause you to lose the game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
Not for the faint of heart!  An (Action) Cube management game!

It can be quite challenging to plan for the activation of cards when you have no control when, nor how many, cubes of the right color will come out!

Single color cards are easier to plan for, two colors doable but more difficult, but 3 and 4 colors?  You really need to plan 5-6 turns ahead for those, and hope the right numbered dies come up, or you have a Joker token or have already activated card(s) which provide you with a cube of the needed color(s).

With this said, it is possible to, or should I say necessary, to go for card combos.  And there are many possibilities.

Not all cards will come up during a game, so there is a lot of replay value.  At the same time, there is a fair amount of randomness (cards, action cube die rolls), which is somewhat mitigated by the fact that there are more than one copy of a card, albeit with different resource costs.  Further, there are always 2 extra cards during the card selection phase, so even the last player will have a choice out of 3 cards.

All in all an interesting game, but somewhat of a brain burner when it comes to planning ahead.

Weight: Medium Heavy (for a euro)

RATING: 7.4
1.每回合抽4張卡片加上2張契約卡，加總決定貢金。
按照玩家人數4/3/2移除0/1/2張卡片。 依順序挑選一張卡片到版圖上。
2.擲6顆骰，使用骰數與顏色放置對應區域，然後將船舵右璇一格。
3.四種行動可以任意選擇:
a.付出資源完成卡片
b.付出資源拿取貨物片
c.付出1/3/5/7前進順位軌1/2/3/4
d.付出任意資源移動船任意格
e.購買分數(一次)
f.使用已完成卡片
4.扣分可能性:
A.當你版圖上的空格都擺滿卡片沒完成，你又拿到第６張卡片時-3分!
B.當轉動輪盤後，你沒有任何資源可以拿時-3分!
C.當遊戲結束時，你有沒完成的卡片時-3分!

RATING: 8
Awesome game.  I like how the dice work, in particular the "time value of actions".  Along with the dice, the cards make the replayability huge.  The rules are simple enough that you can focus on the decisions that matter instead of fighting with understanding rules.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This game is difficult to play well.  You must cope with a large amount of hidden information: you don't know what the dice rolls or building choices will be.  You must develop as many options as possible early, and then pounce on the best opportunities that present themselves later.  There are a number of different building powers with distinct powers, and certain buildings work well with others.  However, you have relatively little control over which buildings you can active, and when you can do so.  Therefore, you must develop your strategy as you play.

Dumb luck could be a factor if you can get a powerful building out early due to dice rolling, but more likely careful planning will earn the victory.

It is difficult to keep track of what other players are doing the first time you play.  They have a variety of colored cubes in many locations, several cards with a variety of colors and powers described in tiny text, a ship moving about with a variety of goods, a turn order marker they can move, and growing district control of the city.  So, it is tempting to play this game "heads down" so to avoid information overload.  However, you can't really win this way unless everyone is doing so.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.1
It's got this Castle of Burgundy vibe going for it--the use of dice to determine action-related choices each round, and the grinding halt of play when it's not your turn. Early on the turns move very quickly, as you only have a few actions available, so they will most likely be spent on acquiring one tile or (if you're lucky) building a building. But by the last few rounds, you'll have 8+ buildings that can possibly be activated once each, a ton of cubes to spend, etc, so each turn will take quite a while.

Planning is kind of mandatory in this game; if you do not have any action cubes to use in a round, you receive a -3 pt penalty. Likewise, each round you must take a [building] card, but you can only hold 5, so if you are full, you must take a -3 penalty and then throw away a card. If you can't build your buildings, you can imagine the penalties will pile up quite quickly. It can be kind of stressful if what you need doesn't turn up (and the fact that you'll have to wait X turns to get X cubes), but since it is happening to everyone, the penalty is at least a wash if it smacks everyone.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
There is a push-your-luck element in the resource acquisition phase (do you grab more resource that will be available in the future or fewer resources that will be available sooner).  There is also an engine building element in selecting cards that work well together and build a better VP creating engine.  There are multiple paths to victory involving privilege, shipping and real-estate.

RATING: 8.34
[b]2 Player Review:[/b]

Macau is definitely my favorite of Stefan Feld's earlier games and I have a little trouble figuring out why. I think it might be because this is his first game that combines several mechanically different scoring mechanisms. The cube allocation based on dice rolls, area control in the city that combines with point scoring resources, and the card play that adds enough randomness to each turn that it keeps you guessing. 

The cards are a double-edged sword in a two-player game however as there is too much unbalance depending on the luck of the draw. Cards you are hoping for when setting up a points engine or need for set collection, might be removed from play. This could cause your winning strategy to fall flat. 

Overall it's a classic that I love, one that manages to make every cube feel meaningful. However it falls short of true Feld greatness because of the randomness of the cards.

RATING: 8
Tried with 2, 3 and 4 players. Macao is totally themeless, but the mechanics alone are sufficient to make this an interesting game. Resource management (of many little cubes) is at the heart of Macao. The coloured-die-rolling mechanism that constrains player decisions on which cubes are available and when, combined with the requirement to pay for many cards with multiple colours of cubes establishes a healthy tension in the game. The tension is further ratcheted up by the races to buy cheap city quarters and to sell wares first at the ports. Perhaps the only aspect of the game which does not seem to work as well as it could is the card drawing: much can turn on which cards come up and when. But, perhaps greater familiarity with card deck would make this less of a concern.

RATING: 5
To call this game dry, long and themeless is to denigrate a decade of dry, long and themeless games that have come from German publishers.  

The dice mechanic is clever and worth a look, it just deserves to be encompassed in a better game.  

Then there is the physical production.  The English translation of the rules and components is so spotty it hard to believe this made it past Rio Grande.  You will spend an inordinate amount of time squinting.  And the "map" is a ludicrous abstraction.  Additionally the turning mechanism which is a critical component is a bit fiddly and not well thought out.

This was a real miss for me.

Its missing a strong theme (there is one here I think just not that strong) and the true angst that can come from crisis management to be close to as good as ITYOTD for me, that is still his best game in my mind, it pretty much has it all.

RATING: 5
Played one game and probably won't be playing another. Mechanically somewhat sound, but at the same time themeless, soulless and just plain boring. Also too long for what it is. Lots of fiddly cube pushing in the final turns. The game masquerades as an economic engine game, but it really isn't one, since the resources you get as income (action cubes) are both random and transient. Components are appropriately boring, non-descript, and hard to read.

I actually liked the dice/action cube allocation mechanism a lot, at least the idea of choosing between getting few resources now, or many later on. However, being coupled with the need to be able to combine specific colors on the same turn, the system falls down flat and makes planning almost impossible. The system of getting harsh penalties for not activating your cards actually just seems to serve to straightjacket people into playing the game the way the designer envisioned. *Having* to take cards, and having to activate them in order to avoid crippling penalties just seems stupid, both mechanically and thematically.

I have never been a big fan of Stefan Feld's designs, and this game fits right in with most of the other ones: Thematically weak beyond belief, procedural, dry. As long as alea seems to favor his games, they're a lost cause as far as I am concerned...

2017-02-19

I finally played Macao again and this time I liked it :D. The friend who explained it last time made at least 3 mistakes with the rules but this time we played it correctly. This friend was the same that explained Notre Dame to me and it that game he also made big mistake. Nowadays, I no longer pay attention to his explanations :D, I love him dearly, but he can´t explain!

The rules aren´t very complex, you can explain them under 15 minutes. Playtime can be long with four players, so I think it is best as a three player game.

The game´s components and art are adequate, but nothing too exciting.

The theme is barely there, but that never stops me from linking a good Euro game.

As any good Feld, Macao offers very interesting decisions (which cards to activate and when, which dice to take, should I buy VPs, should I claim a district? Should I navigate? Should I advance in the wall?). 

Also, as mot Felds, it has some elements that create pressure, in Macao´s case, you are forced every turn to get a card and if you don´t play it you get penalized. I really like this feature. I also like how the wind roses and dice work, they allow you to plan ahead. 

There is considerable luck in the game, with the cards and with the dice, but the idea is to adapt as best as you can to the current circumstances, so it doesn’t bother me much. The luck of the dice affect all players the same, so it is fair. The luck of the cards can be an issue for me, I need to play more times to see. I also need to verify the power level of the cards, but so far it seems the game has many paths to victory.

Reading from the cards is sometimes confusing, I think the English translation is regular in some cases.

Bottom line, Macao is a borderline 7.0 to me, that is, Macao is a good game. I need to play it more times to see if the game is keeper for me (I think it will be) and I also need to try it with Amy. It offers really interesting decisions and the mechanism of getting resources is unique among the games I have.

One of 2009´s favorites.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Macao is intensely chaotic but also egalitarian in a very Euro way - the action cube "income" choices are wildly divergent but offered to all players equally to make the best of as they can.  I'm not aware of this type of chaos having been implemented in quite the same way elsewhere.  Yspahan is probably the closest but then gives a huge advantage to the player who happens to be first on particular turns.  Everyone has access to the same action cubes in Macao, so if you can't manage them as well as the other players it's hard to blame the dice for that - though there is a little risk management in the selection of cards.

The theme is stapled on, of course, but the game mechanics are so innovative I'm not sure how much I care about that.  Time will tell whether Macao's shine will wear off after a few plays, or whether this game of Euro wave-riding will have staying power.

RATING: N/A
Pickup and delivery, with Rondel
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Macao is a strategic dice game with a cool mix of mechanics that will be hard for me to describe. Every turn, dice of six different colors are rolled. Each color corresponds to a different color "action cube" and the number rolled indicates the number of cubes you get and the number of turns it will be until you get them. So if the red die rolls a 4, and you pick it as one of the two dice you get to select, then you set aside 4 red cubes to use 4 turns in the future. You use the cubes to do a number of things - purchase cards that give you bonuses or points, purchase buildings on the board that give you goods, move your ship to where you can trade the goods, and so on. It's hard to say how much control you have in the game. The dice are random, but you select which to use and how far in the future to place them. The cubes you previously selected determine your choices in the present turn. It's like no other game I've played and it is one of those games where you say, "How the fudge do designers create something this intricate and yet so balanced and playable?" Very impressed. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
Definitely felt like a Feld game, with the tightly interlocked moving parts, and the turn order "walk-way".  The new thing here is the "wind rose," where you use dice to allocate resources up to 6 turns ahead of time.  This is the mechanism that makes the game interesting; really, without this, it wouldn't stand out from the rest of the Euro crowd.  In my first play there felt like a lot of luck was involved, but it will take more plays to see if this is too much luck for me.  Also, although the mechanism is fun and interesting at the beginning and middle of the game, I wasn't satisfied with how it was integrated into the endgame.
