=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
[b]Underdeveloped Nonsense[/b]

Yspahan crossed with Duck Dealer, with lots of tiny card synergies thrown in to make turns even more over-the-top. 

The mechanisms in this game have to be seen to be believed. I hope Stefan Feld is laughing at us. Sadly, it's probably not meant to be funny. Basically what you are doing is using dice to strategically draft six varieties of action points while also managing your next six turns. 

Let me repeat that. Six types of action points. Drafted using dice. Allocated for future use spread over six turns. WTF? You then use the action points to purchase cards (that you've already drafted). The cards mostly involve squeezing extra action points and gold coins. You need the cards to get some cash flow going, so that you can consistently buy points to outpace your opponents. The action points may also be used to purchase goods and to move a ship around to deliver goods. The game also hands out "punishment" tokens if you make the slightest misstep. Gee, where have I seen that before? 

Not a bad game, but certainly it cuts close to parody the way Ystari's games have done in the past. This game needs to try a lot harder on the theme; it seems like a lot of potential there was wasted. 

I would give this another try or two. Doubtful that it has legs for me.

RATING: 9

RATING: 7
Macao scales astonishingly well across the full player count, i.e. one of the few Aleas that can be recommended already for 2 players. Be warned though that with 4 players prone to AP, this will get annoying as players can plan a lot on their turn. The unique turning wheel cube allocation mechanic allows planning up to 6 turns in advance in a 12 turn game. In the action round things don't get easier as the same cubes can be used for multiple purposes: shipping, building, turn order, card activations. Dubbed the prototypical JASE by some, I must say that I enjoy it. JASE wouldn't be a negative attribute if all JASEs were like Macao. The length of player moves keeps it from an 8, at least for now.

RATING: 9
I've only played it once so far, but it was a really good game. One of the most ingenious mechanisms I've seen in a game

A lot of the scoring mechanisms and multifaceted decisions are on-par with the standard Stefan Feld experience, but it's the catalyst of Stefan Feld's games of how you make those decision that sets them so far apart. MaCao is no exception to that, and I found it to be one of the most clever systems. The wind rose system provides a system full of tactics, strategy, and an even an element of luck with divergent paths among players. I've never seen anything like it before.

RATING: 7
I wanted to like this a lot more than I did.  The Alea pedigree is the first reason.  Secondly, Stefan Feld has a fairly decent track record (Rum & Pirates and In the Name of the Rose notwithstanding) with me.  I don't even really care too much about the boring and overused theme.

I like the rotating windrose mechanism - it is clever and produces some interesting choices.  However, I feel quite frustrated while playing the game.  I realize that a good chunk of this is due to my playing style (I'm not really a planner) and probably inexperience with the game.  I'm not going to say that luck is too dominant either.  It definitely does play a minor role, but everyone still has to make do with the same dice rolls.  

So my frustration lies in the absurd number and variety of cards.  I don't know what I can do but it is difficult to achieve card synergy in the first place, and even when you do, it is very hard to get the more expensive cards out in time to make those expensive cards pay off.  I also tend to not like games with megaturns where you do a whole bunch of stuff with different cards.  I think it is best done simultaneously if possible (like in Loyang), since a slow player will more rapidly drain the fun out of games like these.  However, I have not yet experienced the ultralong games that some have mentioned.  1.5 hours is about right, though I probably would enjoy this more if it came in under an hour.

I will probably try and play this a couple more times, maybe 2 player with my wife, before I decide the game's fate.

RATING: 10
Excellent game!  The central mechanism -- few goods soon vs. many goods in the future -- is very fresh.  Every game is different depending on the cards that happen to come out, but you can pick from them to try to make some synergy.  And, of course, you don't just get them, you need to line up the right cubes to "activate" them some time in the future.
I also especially like the way that buying a city space combines 3 different considerations into one simple action: what is the cost, which good does it get you, and how does it help you make a long connected line of city spaces.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.62
Fundamentally a good to very good game.  

Interesting use of dice in a Euro to allocate future resources.

There are three annoying/weird things.
The rules translation is annoying and it is very tempting to rewrite it to make more sense in English.

Thematically the fact that you can acquire goods after your ship has set sail and still sell them at their destination port is interesting.

The goods are pictured, but then referenced on some cards by name with no details of which picture matches which description.  Most of the time you can work it out, but how hard would it have been to put the picture of the goods item on the card that matched the tile and the board which only use the graphic image???

Rant over.  Yes these things are irritating, but do not spoil the game or my willingness to replay it.

RATING: 5
I wanted to like this one, but glad I played before I bought it.  There are just too many things I didn't like about it.  One, it seems like the kind of game that should take an hour or 90 minutes. Instead, our three player game took 2.5 hours.  I realize with multiple plays, playing time would decrease but it was just too long for what it was.  The biggest thing is it wants to be a game about long term planning.  But since you have no idea what cards are going to come out it's pretty much impossible.  You just roll the dice and pick the best cubes and hope for the best when the cards come.  Yes, you could take other routes for VP, but the cards are such an integral part of the game.  If you ignore them, you will lose too many points.  So the game forces you to pay attention to cards, then throws them at you randomly each turn.  

I think a good variant and one I'd be willing to try is to draft 10 cards pregame (ala Agricola) or even pass them out randomly.  Then you could see your handful of cards and plan accordingly.  Then the dice rolls wouldn't be as devastating when they didn't quite go your way because you could adjust strategies on the fly. 

In my play, I paid little attention to the turn order track and it hurt me, but I am not sure how badly.  One I got a card where I needed just one white cube to activate the gunner (one GP every turn you're last on the turn order track) but in four straight rolls I couldn't get white it where I needed it.  I should have gone another route to get that elusive cube but being new to the game I didn't think to.

I feel this could be a good game and I imagine my rating would go up with multiple plays.  But it feels like one I'd rather play against strong computer AI than against live opponents.  It would be fun to try different strategies and routes to victory. As it is, the game just takes too long and isn't enough fun to want to pull it out over and over.

RATING: 6
I liked Macao at first, but then it started to feel really restrictive. The strategies are all balanced, so the winner is the person who finds the most synergy with cards and with actions on the board. Sometimes its possible to find synergy easily, sometimes is really just not possible at all. This started to feel a little random. The wind rose is a little too random in that sometimes you need to build a particular card, but the colors simply never line up right, despite you checking for the possibility every turn. Its a good game, if you like Feld you will enjoy it. I'm starting to get a little annoyed with Feld games that require you to plan ahead but then wait for the randomness to allow you to enact your plan, because he usually doesn't give you a way to get around the randomness. Bruges had the same problem for me.

RATING: 9
A Feld's worthy game that's more tactical than usual for him.  Analysis & planning is still possible to a point but is hard.  In that sense it feels a bit like Merkator from Rosenberg but the rewards for analyzing are shallower.  Card text translation is lacking, so read the forums cause there is no FAQ!?!  The rondel cube gathering mechanic is really novel and interesting but ultimately not ground breaking.  It's hard to really screw up and the final scores are generally very close unless someone *really* plays abysmally (very unlike YOTD).  So there is more chance & serendipity involved than in other Feld's game and it's hard to feel firmly in control of your game but it still make for meaningful & satisfying play.  Worth playing to see if it works for you.

RATING: 6
It's a progressive build-up game.  Turns are quicker early in the game and much longer later on in the late rounds as you have more cards already built for use.  It probably has a one-game learning curve as newbies need to get accustomed to planning ahead for large cube collections while managing to have (a) at least a cube for next round and (b) to build on average of, at least, two cards every three rounds.  1:1 ratio would be ideal, of course.  It's one of those a "punishment" games where you need to plan well enough.  (I tend to like In The Year Of The Dragon, Notre Dame and Pillars of the Earth, better b/c they have less of a punishment factor and it takes less time for me to plan/think/analyze.)

RATING: 7
While I thought this was an interesting game, some of my fellow gamers felt less inclined to enjoy the game.  This game is primarily tactical in nature with a little of forward planning but forward planning is often dependent on a finicky dice roll.  Dice are used to determine what goods come out when in the game (you choose two of the dice/goods and put that quantity that many turns ahead).  Cards are central to doing well - if you can cause synergy to occur, the game will flow more easily.  I am curious to see how many different strategies are successful in this game - there are numerous ways to score points between delivering wares, buying VPs based on the 6 card draw of people/office/places cards, and those action cards themselves as well as longest connected network of quarters in the town.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
We got a copy of [thing=55670][/thing] from our very generous Secret Santa. We had been intrigued by the game and were eager to try it, as we had been looking for a "bigger" game for a while. The game has a lot of familiar things going on (area control, resource acquisition, shipping) that are glued together using the unique dice rose mechanism. The other selling point are the hundreds of cards that can give you access to unique abilities. We've generally enjoyed games with lots of cards, so we thought this would be a sure thing. The theme is almost ridiculously generic (just what are these cubes?), but the I wonder if the dice mechanism pays homage to the real life Macao as a gambling center.

In practice, the dice mechanism really constrains the action. It's almost like having to manage a harvest in [thing=31260][/thing] every turn (and is apparently a trademark of Feld's games, of which this is the first we've played). Since you have to allocate the dice rolls for every turn, the game doesn't feel like it has a natural buildup to it--instead, it feels more like a roller coaster where you stock up dice on a certain turn, hit the jackpot and do all your actions, and then wait again. Or maybe we just need to learn the game better---the first game, my wife hated it, but the second game she warmed up to it.  After another game, she really liked it!  After playing the game more, we've been able to use the cards to help mitigate the dice rolls more effectively. For us, it feels like another entry into the "more complicated [thing=3076][/thing]" niche, giving a similar vibe to [thing=30380][/thing], but working better with two players.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Stefan Feld's latest for the alea big box line, Macao, is an interesting game with a somewhat odd structure, and a novel resource management system. While I enjoyed playing, it's a little clunky for reasons I'll get into momentarily, I probably won't buy it for myself, but I'd happily play a friend's copy. I may even give it as a gift to someone I knew would enjoy it.

The most interesting part of Macao is how resources (called action cubes here, since you can use them for more than just buying things) are generated. A mittful of six-siders are rolled part way into each round, each die a different colour. Players choose two dice (and more than one player can choose the same die, which speeds things up a lot), collecting a number of cubes of the die's colour equal to the number rolled on that die. The kicker is, the more cubes you get, the longer you have to wait until you can use them. I think this is a very good resource distribution mechanic, and one which I expect will get used in another game or two (with some twists) in the future.

The problem, for me, lies in how the rest of the systems are largely unconnected to each other. While everything feeds off the action cubes (except for direct purchase of VPs, and even then you usually base gold income from using cubes) Even the cards largely concern resource management and each other, with only a couple that I saw affecting goods & shipping. The only use for gold is to buy lots of VPs (which are set up by a cool mechanic, but sit off in their own little corner). The wall affects turn order, but only a couple of cards, and nothing else, feed back into it. Had the different segments of the game affected each other more directly, the game would feel much more cohesive and less like a sprawling beast.

There is also some issue about how much you can plan in Macao versus being at the mercy of the dice. I actually don't think this is as much of a problem as some commentators, but it can be a bit tricky to figure it all out. In many ways, the planning is a lot like Race for the Galaxy or San Juan: see what you get early and then develop your plan around that, including trying to coordinate cubes so you can bring your buildings out in a timely fashion. I can see how this kind of reactive planning may not be to everyone's taste, though, and adding the purchase of quarters and shipping of goods to this can be a mite overwhelming the first couple times you play.

I think Macao is worth a try for everyone. It's got a bit of strategy, it's got a bit of tactics and it's got a little dice-rolling. When you get down to it, that's a huge chunk of gaming right there. While I rate it a 7 now, there is potential for that moving up with experience, and a better understanding of how to put everything together.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
The idea of getting less action cubes sooner or more cubes later is pretty cool, but the penalty for not having cubes each round makes it less so in execution. I wish they had instead made it so you have stuff that makes taking less cubes earlier potentially more profitable if you can use them to set up a card engine sooner. Speaking of the cards, I really don't like them. They are all pretty situational, and while you can build an engine with them it feels the other half ofthe time you are just getting rid of them to avoid the penalty for having too many. The rest of the game is a cacophony of thematically boring different scoring methods, ranging from the not too shabby race to deliver goods first to the awful gold to points conversion that adds fiddliness for something that adds about one important decision (gold converts usually at about a 1 to 2 ratio to points, but do I need to start taking worse conversions since otherwise the game will end before I can convert them all?).

RATING: 9
It is very important to play first. 
Is there randomness? yes, it is there to every player. 
Everybody gets the same dice and the same cards to choose from. 
If you play first, you choose the cards first, you get to move your ship first, and to buy a quarter first.
Then, there is a great chance you'll win the game.

There is also one more thing as much important as to play first. Making good plan when choosing the right dice, specially in the first rounds. If you manage to choose well your dice, but not playing first, you still have good chance to win, since there is a lot going on at the sea and at the city to compensate that.

RATING: 8
One of the better games Stefan Feld has created. Introduction of a new game mechanism, the "goods wheel", that later appeared in other games.

Thematically appealing (for a Feld!). Lots of variation because of the large amount of cards that can be used, that influence the game and your strategy,  each time you play it.

What annoys me a bit is that while playing, certainly during the first few rounds, you are driven by your board with cards. Since you need to make sure your board doesn't get full, you need to collect the resources you need to activate your cards. This is sometimes such a dominant mechanism, that you cannot really develop a strategy, and all you do is "think minimal". Many times the "think minimal" when selecting cards is very effective. 
It could be I don't play this game very well, but to me, this seems like a flaw.

Also, it is quite a solitaire game. Once someone starts using his cards, it's hard to follow what he is doing, since you cannot see what's on the small cards, and actually, you couldn't care less. So everybody is looking at his own cards. Only point of interaction is in delivering goods first and being first on the play order track. Some competition in the city area, but minimal.

Minus: 3 (solitaire, hard to follow what others are doing) - 8 (both, see above)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
An odd yet pleasing game. Unlike a plethora of other designers, Stefan Feld is apparently not willing to just mix up another variation of time-honored ingredients, but strives for genuinely new stuff with each new game. While this is laudable (and ensures that the new Alea games feel less like inferior versions of Alea classics than a whole lot of other new heavy Eurogames), it also makes your first play of one of his games more of a stumble than a run.

This gets better in subsequent attempts, but even then, the resource scarcity and the challenge of getting the right stuff at the right time probably ensures that the machine you build up in Macao will always be a rather clanky and cranky one. Unlike the machine of the game itself, which runs smoothly and intiutively in spite of its originality.

RATING: 4

At the end of the game I played, I could not look back and see any clear decision that I should have made differently that did not depend on some random outcome to make it meaningful or not.

RATING: 5
Of the Feld games I've played as of my first play of Macao (Notre Dame, In the Year of the Dragon, and The Speicherstadt), this is the most opaque. It's challenging, though not overly complex, but I don't like that opacity.

In the other three, it was clear at the start of the first play what I should be doing, or at least clear enough to give me a shot at doing something productive. Macao felt like learning to juggle while taking a phone call and balancing on a rope over quicksand, and I didn't love it. I suspect I'd like it better with one less thing to manipulate, or one less layer, but I already have Feld games like that.

I'd play it again, but I can't see why I'd pull it out over any of my other Feld titles unless I had an opponent or group who was excited about it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
[i]Macao[/i] is a great game that just might be better than [i]Castles of Burgundy[/i].  Although there are many ways to score points, don't believe those who call it "VP soup".  Not all options are equally good and a bad player will get crushed in this game way worse than in CoB.

----------

After a couple more plays, I definitely think Macao is better than CoB.  Why is it rated lower, then?  I think there are two main reasons:

(1) CoB is a more streamlined game.  In Macao, there are a lot of cards that have to be read, which slows the game down if you haven't played before.  Also, the game play is somewhat fiddly.  For example, having to draw 6 cards, add up the values for the tribute offering, then remove a card (in a three-player game) is kind of funky and takes some getting used to.  Also, there is a bit more downtime when others are selecting a card to add to their tableau.

(2) Macao may overwhelm some with the number of possible choices.  There are 15 ways to choose 2 of 6 dice and within each die choice there are many considerations as to how to use cubes of that color.  Do you get your money engine going, buy a card, buy a goods tile, advance your boat and/or advance in turn order?  Do you take fewer cubes for an immediate purpose that is known or do you take a bunch of cubes for down the road perhaps not knowing what they will be used for?

----------

I now think this is Feld's masterpiece.  If this and Trajan were published in reverse order, Macao would be ranked higher.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
12/20/11 Played a four-player game. 
What are these 300 cubes in six colors supposed to represent? They aren't money, or goods, or units of time, or influence, or armies, or workers, or victory points, or anything else I've ever run into in a Eurogame. So I skimmed the eight most popular reviews of the game on BGG and not a single reviewer said what they were. Nor do the rules posted to BGG. Apparently they have NO real life counterpart. As best as I can tell they're just something the designer threw in to add "complications" to the game for players to overcome.

Macao is not a terrible game if you can get past the main feature of the game, the action cubes, are never explained. I couldn't. I pretty much mentally wrote the game off five minutes into the explanation. The cubes were six flavors of pixie dust as far as I was concerned. So the whole game felt "silly" to me. The interesting way they were obtained via dice roll, how they were used, the novel rotating wind rose, and the cards that gave certain advantages were merely delicious toppings put on a pizza made of concrete; pointless. 
I also took issue with the silly abbreviations on the cards (AC, GC, PP), That's being opaque for no reason. 
Lastly, being able to receive goods on your ship after its left Macao is thematically ridiculous.

RATING: 7
A unique dice mechanic where dice each representing different resources are rolled and then players can choose two of the resources.  If you choose a 5 than you get 5 of that resource but the trick is that you don't get them until 5 turns from when they're chosen.  Likewise a 2 will give you 2 of that resource 2 turns from now (this is kept track of on a wheel).  Players can choose the same resources as other players. 

There are also cards and a number of ways to generate VPs on the board.  Players are trying to time their resources on the wheel in order to cash in (you must spend all your resources on the turn you receive them).  

I really need to try this again.  Rating could move up or down.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Underestimated and overlooked gem of a medium-weight Euro.  Dice randomness provide euro resource cubes... can get one right, or up to six in the future... really loving that mechanic more and more.  And the gameplay is TIGHT!  Players' decisions constantly affect others' play, from card drafts to wall position to city tiles bought.  Surprisingly high affordance... a simple glance at the board will show you all the pertinent info!  Only downside is that the law/people/bldg card text can sometimes be ambiguous.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Can't tell you why, because I shouldn't, but I LOVE this game. Part of it is probably the combination of long and short term planning, and the great replayability due to the different cards coming out in each game... Oh, and then there's the 6 different color dice rolling!

As a BIG plus, this game works great with 2, 3 or 4 players. Not many multiplayer Euros work that well (or are really meant) for two players.

In conclusion: I highly recommend this game to experienced gamers... even though the theme is a complete paste-on, you're still pushing wooden cubes and it is not (in my experience) something your non-gaming friends will enjoy.

I give it a 9/10.

RATING: 6
Played once with four people.  Players collect cubes, which they then use to build buildings or carry out other actions.  While the cube-collection mechanism is novel and interesting.  On each turn 6 dice are rolled, one for each color of cube, and players can pick two of the dice: the number on each die represents the number of cubes a player gets of that color and how many turns in the future the player gets access to those cubes.  

Unfortunately, however, this die mechanism makes the game extremely luck heavy: players need to get the exact number of cubes needed for each building on one turn.  Thus, if a building needs 2 of one color and 1 of another, they have to rely on the dice to make that possible.  Combine this with a random selection of buildings available each turn, each of which give a variety of special powers, and you have one very complicated resource generating / building-building game.

To succeed, players needed to build buildings that had powerful actions (and could build on each other); given than getting the building requires luck (random buildings are chosen from each turn), and getting the cubes requires luck, this means that the game as a whole required a lot of luck.  

The game took more than three hours; it was not worth the time given how luck heavy it is (a player has to both get buildings that will amplify each other, then get the cubes to build those buildings, and then be able to carry out the actions that will be amplified).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
I just played this again after trading it away several months ago, and it is even worse than I remembered. The "strategy" here is completely transparent and scripted: do little bit of everything, and you will win. If other players do the same, then you might lose, but you won't be able to pin it on anything anyone did; it probably came down to whoever happened to get a sweet card draw when they were going first or cashed in on the first 3:1 VP to gold exchange rate you've ever seen.

The overflow of mechanisms in this game provide an illusion of complexity, but anyone spending more than 30 seconds on his turn is wasting time. Basically, all you do is get as many contiguous properties early in the game as you can, being sure to block others from creating such chains. Generally go for the max dice in the beginning of the game and the dice you need to activate all your cards late in the game. By default pick the cheapest cards so you can get them off your tableau fast, but do grab a couple "Game End" score cards early in the game and play to those as best you can. Cash in when the ratio is 2 VP to 1 gold or better. Then ship your ass off. That's it. Sadly, this is not "easier said than done"; it is in fact quite easy and rather boring. Really don't need to play this one again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6

"Macao, the mysterious harbor city on the south coast of China, is the Portuguese trading center in the far east at the end of the 17th century. The players take on the roles of the energetic adventurers who sought their fortunes in Macao. Whether as captain or governor, as craftsman or scholar, numerous exciting functions are offered the players. Who will use his various possible actions the wisest? Who will have the best plan and can acquire the most prestige by the end of the game?"

Yes, that really is the game description. Yes, the game really comes with 300 cubes. No, they don't even bother to paste a theme on the different colors, referring to them instead with such exciting names as "Blue Action Cube" or the catchier "Blue AC" for short. Yes, the entire game is pushing ACs around in order to earn more GCs [that's "Gold coins"... duh] with your B/P cards ["Building/person"... yes, the "Midwife" has the same scruffy Euro sailor icon] so you can score more PPs ["Prestige points"... victory wha?]. Yes, the game uses all of those abbreviations.

Despite all this, the game has one redeeming feature [no, it's not that it includes a regular heptagon as a game component] in the form of the action cube allocation system. Every round one die is rolled in each of the six AC colors [damn, I'm doing the abbreviation thing now... as if "action cube" wasn't abstract enough]. The number on the die indicates not only how many cubes you get in that color, but how many turns you must wait before you get them. The greedier you are, and the more actions you want to take using that color cube, the longer you will have to wait. It's kind of a reverse J. Wellington Wimpy offer: "I'll give you a cheeseburger Tuesday, unless you want grilled chicken... then you have to wait 'til Wednesday."

The game penalizes you every turn you're not able to do something, so you often have to sacrifice and satisfice. It's no use taking the promise of 6 beautiful BACs [Black Action Cubes... or was it Blue?] 6 turns from now if you don't have a single AC, much less a GC, to your name to burn this turn for PP [?]. Playing the odds and building a diverse enough economic engine so that you always have the right colors in the right quantities at the right time is a Euro-optimizer's wet dream. I mean, seriously. OMG STFU 2 BAC + 3 VAC W/ THE B/P & 6 GC 4 12 PP FTW! OMG OMG OMG! UWE! UWE! WOWWWEEEE!! [Oops, sorry... got a little carried away]. The entirety of the remainder of the game is all but worthless, however. Stefan Feld is the master of clever unremarkable games, and this is probably the cleverest and... unremarkablest? This would make a great filler; as a full game, it rings hollow.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
In the old Portugese city of Macao, there are 12 turns. Each player first chooses a card to put on their tableau. 6 different colored dice are then rolled, and players choose two of the colored dice: the number of pips on the dice are the number of action cubes in that color the player receives, but that number of turns later, so some planning is required. Players use action cubes to activate cards, use activated cards, take territories, move on the wall (for turn order in choosing cards) or moving ships to sell goods. Some of the cards requirent different colored cubes to activate, and at the end of the game, non activated cards count against you. 

There are many things to consider, and I like the game. It might be a great game if it could be shorter. There are many possibilities of tactics that requires more play to uncover.

RATING: 10
It's a pretty fast one, maybe faster than CoB, which is a plus. After reading the rules, I was concerned that there wouldn't be enough action cubes to do anything, but it turns out you accumulate so many on the last turn you could sail all around the world and back. Like Trajan, you have to match up the colored cubes on the given turn in order to activate things, but there are 2 things to activate (cards and Macao city quarters) vs the 1 Trajan tile in Trajan. Much of the flavor of the game comes from the interaction of the activated card abilities, and these would seem to vary a great deal game to game, enough to make this eminently replayable. Like CoB and In the Year of the Dragon, there's the Feld variable turn order mechanic at play. Like the former (and unlike the latter), not being able to go first in this game does not necessarily mean that you lose. I like this more than Trajan because there is more randomization turn to turn with the rolls of dice. 
------
Second play with 4 took a lot longer than the first play with 3. So I may have to take back what I said about it being a fast one. But everything else is still true!
------
All of the above mostly true on a third play, although it seems that the "double price for specific ware delivery" cards have won the winner the game each time.
------
Like the best games, Macao just seems to get better with age.  Either that or it hits its sweet spot around plays 4-8!
------
After 15 plays or so, I unabashedly appreciate this game. The flavor comes from the card interplay, and there are so many different strategies to explore because of that, much more than the relatively obvious double price ware strategy. Upping from 9.

RATING: 7
This game should have been language independent. It would be so much faster and easier just looking at the cards seeing symbols instead of text and being able to know immediately what each card does.
There is some luck in the beginning when you almost blindly select resource cubes, and whether a certain color combination of cubes that would match the cards that turn up later would be possible within a few rounds. Later on once you have the economy going it is much less of a problem and planning is easier, so the game is strategic enough. The game reminds me of through the ages (thankfully with managable playing time here, but with AP types it could get longish(3 hours)) a bit with the card picking and activation, and surely somewhat Notre Dame with the active cards providing money and VPs. Overall it seems to be a solid title with good replayability.

RATING: 5
Not that impressed with the game. With all of the different cards, there can be some synergy, but with the randomness of the draw, you can really get cards late game that are completely worthless. The fact that I can't clearly say how I can improve my game or what mistakes I made during play tells me that I'm not going to want to play this often, but I wouldn't refuse to play.

RATING: 8
A pinch of risk-management, a sprinkle of economic engine, and a fair bit of card-combo thrown in. While the dice engine at the heart of the game is enchanting, I'm not 100% convinced it works quite as well as the designer intended. It can be immensely frustrating too, with everyone's game easily cursed by poor dice rolls and card draws, and made worse by the sloppy English translation.

Further Thoughts: I'm struggling to credit this as a cohesive game any more. It can be very tiresome struggling with wonton dice and watching an opponent clocking up dumb-ass-massive combos without being able to do anything about it. There is an interesting degree of balance in the game. But there's less GAME in the game than I had anticipated. Downgraded from 7 to 6.

RATING: 7
[i]Macao[/i] needs several plays before all the options and possibilities will gel, but it repays the effort. It is not simple to teach (Stefan Feld, so no surprises there). 

Much like Feld's [i]In the Year of the Dragon[/i], [i]Macao[/i] is deep. It presents complicated long term planning decisions and relatively simple short-term choices. RATING: N/A
A great game! We have only played Macao 2-player as of yet It is wonderful as a 2-player experience but I suspect with more players the dynamics of the game will really shine! The mechanics are really well integrated and the board and components are awesome! The cards...yes the cards...really make the games replay ability different every time! The options of card choices are quite varied. However there is a bit of luck in what cards you may be able to procure; similar to the settler phase in Puerto Rico (i.e. in PR you may really want that coffee plantation but if your opponent takes it...you're out of luck!) Same here! 

Of course, the Action dial is one of the most fun and challenging mechanics of the game! Rolling 6's gives you a lot of Action Cubes on the dial, but you have to wait so long to use them...intense! Finding time and AC's to allow you to move your ships is tricky also...but essential to the game! And what about that Prestige point track...push your luck...or bite the bullet and pay your hard earned Gold Coins for Prestige Points...what a game!

RATING: 8.25
規則:
*版圖左邊隨機放置所有貨物，joker版塊要放在深色區域，office卡放版圖周遭，每一回合兩張，其他卡牌勻成牌庫
*每個玩家起始5元，從牌庫抽人數加兩張，逆序挑一張放在個人版圖上，接這正序，每個玩家擺選一個顏色方塊放在輪盤一的位置，選另外一個顏色的兩個方塊放在輪盤二的位置
*遊戲進行12輪，每輪三個階段
1.抽牌階段 牌庫抽四張加兩張office卡決定這回合的錢換分比例，跟據玩家數移出對應抽的牌直到等於玩家數，照順位拿一張牌放在個人圖板上
*當拿第六張牌到圖版時要棄一張並且拿扣分三標記
2.擲骰階段 擲所有骰，每個玩家選擇兩顆骰(可重複所以可同時做)，放對應顏色的放塊到對應輪盤位置上，接下來轉輪盤一格，如果此時箭頭位置沒有方塊，拿扣三分標記
3.行動階段 玩家輪流做完所有想做行動，可做行動有
A 支付卡片費用啟動他，將卡片移到個人圖版外，上面功能每回合可用
B 支付費用拿城市版塊 每輪只能拿一次並放下個人標記
C 支付1/3/5/7/...移動牆順位軌1/2/3/4/...格
D 支付N方塊，移動船N格，格子不互卡，在港口可以卸貨
E 用錢換分數 只能做一次
F 使用啟動的卡片
G Pass
*遊戲結束，每張版圖上的卡拿扣三分標記，有分數的啟動卡加分，城市的最大連接數乘兩分，平手順位前面的獲勝

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
For an alleged dice game, it surprised me with how long and thinky it is. First there's the card draft, where you're trying to obtain cards that you'll be able to both build quickly and which have an ability that complements your other cards. Building the cards however requires you getting cubes in various of the 6 colours. The dice mechanism is neat and makes life interesting. Do you take small numbers of cubes for use in the next round or 2 and get immediate use, or do you take lots of cubes for use many turns in the future and get massive benefit then. The latter seems typically better, especially as the more powerful cards requires different colours, and you need to arrange it so you receive cubes in each of those colours on the 1 turn - easy if the dice for those colours all roll the same number, but usually not so easy and you'll want to give yourself as many turns for it to happen as you can. Investing in cards that allow you to manipulate your cube selections or requirements may be useful here. Choosing the 2 colours of cubes you'll take can be a lengthy process, as you're given other options to spend those cubes and acquire victory points - buy a goods tile (get the right colour cubes in the right amount again!), move your ship to eventually deliver the goods tiles before other players, convert cubes into cash and acquire straight up VP's, adjust player order to get better card selections. Lots of things to do, lots of things to think about. It's very solitaire. I've usually got no idea what abilities the other players are using to get along. Another player might buy a goods tile I was eyeing off, or beat my ship to a harbour before I can sell towards the end of the game, or take the card I want in the draft but that's about the extent of the interaction. Almost your entire focus is worrying about getting the right cubes together at the right time to build your cards - and then spending the excess cubes on the rest. It's a game I've enjoyed for this puzzle aspect, and exploring different complementing card strategies, but it's a very quiet game, very Euro.

RATING: N/A
A slow-building open-information game with card tableau-building, a bit of pick-up-and-deliver, and some path-building, all to obtain PP. Much tactics with attempts for strategy. Some of the game is to alleviate obtaining the "-3 PP" penalties. I love the wind-rose, and the dice-shared mechanism. Boy, does my wife kick my butt at this game!

Game play basics are easy to learn and teach. It is playing that has the Learning Curve due to the "I've no idea what to do or how to strategize" factor. This Feld game is different because instead of being the typical Feldian-Point-Salad game, it is a "Here, breath through this straw for the next 80 minutes" game.

RATING: 7.5
Cool mechanics, but frustrating (in a good way). Based on the dice, you collect cubes in order to play on future turns and use these cubes (like money) to move your ship, deliver goods, do actions and activate cards. It is tough to get what you need, so make sure to not chose too many really good cards that you will not be able to use as they are tough to activate (due to the mix of cubes needed or large cost in cubes). Well worth a try and a great addition to the Alea series. The one thing to note is that the design is not the best. The cards always refer to other cards in the game, but icons are used on the cards while words on the text, so you have to ensure you are taking the right card if you are going for a combo (but this is not a major part of the strategy).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The windrose/dice mechanism in this game is a very ingenious mechanic forcing players to judge the balance in getting fewer resources now versus more later.  But, boy could this game use some better components.  The same drab artwork is on every person card as well as every buidling card.  C'mon.  Lady Violet should be sexy, not some fat guy with a beard. For me, the game was good for about 15 plays or so, but my taste for Feld's games has diminished.  Feld used to be a common name on my game shelf, but now I am down to one.  Macao is a decent game overall, it's just that my tastes have gone in a different direction.

RATING: 9
Macao is a hot little euro, in the historical shipping-goods-to-markets genre.  Its is deeper than Stefan Feld's other games (that I've played).  It has one of the nicest implementations of a rondel I've seen.  Is it thematic? Yeah right.

It does has a sweet mix of long reaching strategic decisions and tactical opportunities.  There is a lot of chance in the game, for sure, but there is also much that players can do.  Small opportunities, when taken advantage of, produce rewards.  Chance can interfere with all your plans, forcing you to improvise.  General Eisenhower was probably describing this very game when he declared [i]"Planning is essential, but plans are utterly useless"[/i].

The way the dice come out affects the character of the game: a lot of big rolls early will create a very different game than if a lot of small rolls come out early.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This game feels like other Stefan Feld designs in that you end up so focused on scrambling to avoid penalties that you sometimes forget to actually try to score points.  The dice do provide some randomness, but since everyone uses the same dice (and you can use dice "selected" by other players), it doesn't end up being biased.  Overall, the game is very tense, and trying to balance short-term and long-term objectives takes center stage.

(5+ plays)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I enjoy Macao, but it has some issues that I cannot understand how Stefan didn't see (outside of lack of play testing). Nice mechanism with the dice;  Neat to have to balance more, but delayed, resources with less, but immediate, resources.  Suffers a bit from down time issues as each player has to optimize a strategy on their turn.  The end game is rather flawed & borderline broken:  you can lose 3 points from a bad draw of the cards (which you can't plan for); if you have a money-engine based strategy, you can be shafted by low tribute prices at the end;  leftover money is worth nothing at the end either;  several times I have been annoyed that I planned for 'average' tribute prices & then it is randomly cheap, allowing other players to get more points that they couldn't have otherwise afforded & then my leftover cash is worthless.  The wall-based turn-order mechanism is also flawed due to the need to often sacrifice cubes on the dice-selection to get the color one needs; making some folks cube poor & other cube rich; thus the rich get richer because they can select earlier.

I like the mechanics, but the sum of the parts is perhaps not better than the parts itself. 

Need to play more.

Theme is artificial. Don't like abstracted maps. There is some detail in the presentation of the theme (the cards) but it doesn't really help.

A lot of damage controll - you mainly activate cards not to get hurt, the functions of the cards are second prio. I don't think that there can be so much real long term strategy involved. The variarity of the cards and all it's possible implications suggest otherwise, though...But many of the end-bonus-cards could be impossible to draw profit of even if you draw it early on: the other cards you need for the end-score-bonus might just not show up at all, at least not all of them that is required for your bonus - I think over 40 cards are not used in the game. And even if you are first to choose, in a game less with less than 4-players, some cards that you want to grab could be just thrown away. Anyhow, the cards you get to deal with creates variation from game to game and I think that it should be hard to rely on "pet-strategies". And that is the up-side of course...

But it is a fun game - or, it is pleasant to play.

RATING: 7
I'm not normally a fan of Feld's designs, with their convoluted point-collecting optimization mini-games, but I rather like Macao. It has some superfluous mechanisms, but is otherwise quite tight (at least, for a Feld).

It has a very interesting mechanism for gaining cubes, and then a couple of ways to spend them for points, which are not all that interesting. The fact that you have some control, but not a whole lot, over which cubes you get which turn, makes that you can't optimize everything perfectly. A bit like Trajan, in that regard, with perhaps less tension. It sounds like a somewhat artificial limitation on player options and perfect planning, and I guess in the end that's what it is, but in practice it's quite fun. It doesn't feel frustrating when you can't get what you want, and it's exciting when everything works out.

Macao isn't a game I'd go out of my way for to play, but with its dice rolling, wind rose turning, card collecting and engine building, playing Macao is a pleasant experience.

RATING: 7
This is a fun early Feld, where we see the inspiration for many future games. You roll dice to determine resources, you collect sets of tiles, you have the turn order stack mechanic he puts into his games, and you have an early version of his card combo mechanic you see in Bruges. It's an interesting game to play, and it's nice in how uncomplicated it seems compared to his later designs. Sometimes returning to the basics is refreshing. I didn't hold onto this as I have many of the games that overlap the mechanics, but I certainly enjoyed playing it.

:star: Innovative mechanics

:star: Good with min or max players
:star: Low time between turns
:star: Comfortable complexity
:star: Re-playability
:star::nostar::nostar: Enjoyment

RATING: 5
It has a nice core mechanic, where the dice system creates a pool of available options - the further out the more options. The windrose is a nice way to represent those future options. The card drafting is as much about surviving as giving you an opportunity. It is still a point salad where you are collecting resource chits to score either by delivering on building a chain on the board. Converting resources to points, juggling starting order which is more important because of the limited card draft. At least there is significant competition on the central board.

While you may be able to get one strategic option going, much of it seems to be tactically driven base on the limited card draw. You spend time planning your risks, and if the dice don't come about in the right order you're hosed. 
I see a lot of what went into Trajan in this one. While I liked the concept behind the windrose and the cube allocations, I can wrap my head around the roundcala and the tile draws in Trajan better. I can see why this is popular, however, I'd prefer to play Trajan

End of the day - it just confirms my opinion that I'm not particular fond Feld's designs.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
R.A says how I feel about this game exactly "I haven't felt this let down by a game in a long time. Perhaps I came into it with expectations that were too high-- my eternal fandom for 'In the Year of the Dragon' made any Stefan Feld an auto-buy for me. But, much like the tedium of the four hour bore-fest called Loyang, Macao is extraordinarily procedural.

My favorite feeling in a game is the tension from having way to much to do and not enough time to do it; I love that perfect and by-design torture of being pulled in twenty directions... wanting, no needing(!) to do it all. Right. Now.

Part of the frustration I felt with Macao is that for the first two to three rounds you do feel that gamey-goodness. Macao teases you, lulls you into thinking that you are about to play something great. But the longer the game progresses-- most oppressively in the last third of the game-- the more the choices are dictated by capricious dice rolls that collide (for better or worse) into an engine that is already too built to change.

A game like Macao offends by constraining your choices, squeezing them down narrow pathways until your work as a gamer is to simply eliminate untenable options and pick from the leftovers. While the compass-rose-dice-wheel-thing is fresh, it is also dictatorial: your choices are so narrowly determined by the fickle hand of fate that later, once you have access to the action cubes, the allocation feels limited and prescribed. 

In effect what this means is that the game comes down to drafting cards. Yes, you want to get some synergy. But the choices in each round of card drafting are also narrow. If you are pursuing a particular path because of what you have built already, the card drafting too is often obvious. This leaves the "game" in allocating dice rolls for action cubes. But here again the choices are constrained. You have to fill out all the empty spots or get dinged with negative points. And when you are free of that threat, you are left with this:
"One gray, two black are useless-- although four gray would have been great, to bad that isn't what was rolled-- I guess that leaves 4 green or 3 red... yawn."

Macao let me down not because it is a bad game, but because it gives the illusion-- but not the substance-- of a thinky, tense game. You want to build this fabulous engine and tweak the motor for maximum effect, but because the course you set for yourself often clashes with the confining and fixed possibilities of the dice, all that is left is for the monkey to pull the levers and for the engine to run itself."

RATING: 1
Nov. 2016: From 2 to 1.

Torture.

Three word strategy: Pick big dice.

The rondel could be substituted with random cube draw.

==========================

Nov. 2011: This game rubbed me the wrong way.

It's a kitchen-sink Euro (like a lot of Euros coming out now). There's card drafting, auctioning for turn order, territory expansion, pick-up-and-deliver, and cube-pushing resource management. Not my kind of game, but so far nothing too offensive.

What really annoys me is the mechanic used to get new resource cubes. Simply put, if you want more resources of one kind, you must choose to receive them at later turn. Because resources are so scarce, the best move is usually get cubes later in the game. This by itself is fine. During the game, you draft special ability cards. You activate these cards with these resources. This itself is fine. You could draft the card first then try to get the required cubes later.

What really, really annoyed me is the luck fest involved in getting the correct cubes at the right time. I won't explain the cube/rondel mechanic here, but simply put, you do not have a free choice of which resources you want at which particular turn. The choices that you do have are determined by dice, and the options available are usually very, very restrictive. For example, during one turn, if you want red cubes, you can only get them at one particular turn sometime in the future. And if you need those cubes at a particular turn, then you are at the mercy of the dice.

To me, the game amounted to planning ahead (meaning getting lots of resource cubes that will appear later in the game) for something you can't plan for -- a special ability card with the appropriate activation cost (in cubes).

RATING: 7
Macao combines just enough luck with just enough skill to deliver an incredibly satisfying experience and intrigued me on anything else Feld has designed. Plus... it has goods delivery, which I have always been and always will be a sucker for.

Pros:
+ Amazingly calibrated balance of luck and skill.
+ Rewards forward thinking and strategic play, but also demands something of a tactical mind when executing built up turns and positioning on the wall.
+ Beautiful, simple components.
+ Goods delivery!
+ Many ways to generate victory points.
+ Cards provide a different game every time and help shape your strategy. The synergy between different cards can be incredibly satisfying.

Neutral:
[b]o[/b] Not newbie friendly. While the game has luck a skilled player will nearly always TROUNCE a new player and it takes a couple games to get the hang of things.

Cons:
- Pretty fiddly and you'd probably better either bag each type of cube separate or do what I did and buy some Plano boxes.

RATING: 6
I can certainly see how some people would really like it. It's fun. It's kind of crazy and although it uses dice, there is not really that much luck. Macao has twelve turns and during turn six, I was thinking that I'd rate Macao a nine. It was fun to pick the dice to get the action cubes and to plan out what kind of actions I'd need for future turns. But about turn nine, I realized that Macao doesn't build the way I'd like a game to build. Usually, games start out slow and as the game goes on, players have more resources and can do more cool things. Because at the end of the game all of the dice are essentially ones, you only get one action cube to place. Earlier in the game, when you can collect large numbers of cubes, you can't possible know what cards you will have access to at the end or what cubes you will need to plan for these final turns. You do get to collect a pool of the action cubes ahead of time but without knowing which cards will appear at the end of the game. 

Additionally, as the actions you can take decreases, you've got a ton of cards to activate, so each player's turn takes a long time while they aren't really deciding anything they didn't already decide to do six turns ago. The end game is time consuming, boring and it squashed any excitement that I had at mid game.

RATING: 6.9
A simple points scoring game. You have various methods of scoring points using resource cubes. The resources are a selection from a random set and arrive after you have chosen then, the more you choose the longer the delay. You can use them to purchase victory points in various ways. The most immediate is taking over districts in a city to form an enclave, the least immediate is selling the goods from that to far off places. In between is converting your cubes to gold and buying victory points on a fluctuating basis, or purchasing development cards (worth effectively 3 each but they give bonuses to other things). 
The mechanisms are easy to pick up but the strategy is more difficult, you can focus on one aspect or find synergies between different parts.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This used to be one of my favorite Felds - and I still think it's a good game, but it's definitely gone down a bit in my estimation recently, which was confirmed by a recent play. (I rated this 8 for a long time.) The mechanics, while relatively simple, are as always with Feld, completely original and lead to tough decisions. But Macao is a little fiddly what with all the cards, not to mention he cubes which you have to keep in 7 clear different sections as you rotate that wheel in the middle of them (if only this game were to get a deluxe edition with fixed sections for the cubes and a pointer you could rotate!). And ultimately I find it less enthralling than many other Feld games (chiefly Trajan, Luna, Aquasphere, Amerigo and Bora Bora). It's a good game, but I do think it lacks a certain something.

RATING: 6
Quick playing (4 new players finished in under 2 hours) engine development game.  Has definite phases - the slow initial phase where you think nothing will get done, the middle accelerating phase as things start to come together, the final rush to finish and maximize points.  Lots of cards with variable powers are the key to the various paths one might exploit to victory.  But with so many options, one does need to stay focused on a few paths for maximal benefit.  First playings likely to seem more random due to all the new cards, dice, etc in the game.  But with familiarity, will settle into a more structured game with choices.

RATING: 7
Macao gets a bit fiddly in the final couple of turns - especially if you've stored a lot of cubes and have a lot of plans. I think I like the game quite a bit. More than Notre Dame with its multiple methods of collecting points and resource generation through cards.

Interaction is fairly gentle. There's the positioning on the wall building to allow you to grab better cards, build city spaces before others and get your deliveries in before your opponents. Other than that there's bugger all interaction, but a lot of waiting around for other players to run through their actions. If players plan while others do then you can keep the pace up, but the game does drag in places and it suffers as a result.

I like the resource wheel idea in Macao. I like that all players select from the same roll. I like the planning ahead with limited information of which cards are coming up. I like the different strategies you can plan ahead for. Sure I feel like an accountant doing it, but for a fairly dry game it gets my juices flowing. It's a bit of a puzzle.

The wife doesn't like this game. Too fiddly and hard to keep track of everything she's entitled to on her cards and what others are entitled to on theirs. She had some interesting ideas about improving the design of the game to better keep track of the bits and actions. I think with a bit of self organisation you can separate your every turn and building entitlements with your end of turn and specific cube entitlements. When you get a system you can manage it fairly easily. You have to keep an eye on what your opponents are picking up too, but only if you want to do well.

Can't work out whether this game is a 7 or an 8 for me. Afterwards it feels like a 7 - the next morning it feels like an 8.

RATING: 10
My absolute favorite crisis management game.  It has many different avenues to collect points with lots of tough, important choices.  Do you use your cubes to move you ship closer to a port to deliver your goods, or do you use them to get further along the turn order wall or do you use them to take over a city quarter?  Amidst all of these decisions, you have to choose whether you want less cubes now or more cubes later and have to keep your tableau moving as every card you acquire above 5 is -3 points.  This may go up to a 10 with additional plays.  Excellent depth, controlled chaos, and oodles of fun!

Update following my first 4p game.  Wow, I didn't think it could get better, but it did.  It is incredible.  Macao is just one of those games that I can't get enough of.

RATING: 8
Macao --

Played once; three-player.

I love the choice the wind-rose forces:  a few resources soon, or many later.  A nice mix of strategy and tactics, with the luck of the dice preventing strategy from playing too strong a role.  Conversely, the luck can be a problem if you don't acquire at least one of the cards which helps mitigate luck.  Turn order can be quite important in acquiring key cards, but once you've established a card engine (activated cards whose powers  interact reliably), then turn order becomes merely a matter of convenience.

I like Macao a lot.  If we had unlimited space, I wouldn't hesitate to add this to our collection.  As our crowded shelves currently stand, I find it difficult to devote space to a rather slow-playing game we would likely play only infrequently.

[i]Update 07 Nov 2010:[/i]  After deciding we didn't need to add the game to our collection, Rich and I found ourselves talking about the various mechanics with such admiration that we realized it does indeed belong on our shelves after all.  The dice selection / wind rose mechanic is the most novel feature (with some similarity to Yspahan, I admit), but the other features (like the changing value of turn order, and the collection of tokens in the city quarters, and delivery to matching ports) are notably well-balanced.  The card powers can be unfair, but they bring such interesting interactions.  After our first game, Eric showed us some cards which had not happened to come up, and we found ourselves looking forward to seeing how they would affect play.  So long as we commit to playing our turns quickly, this game may hit the table more often than we originally anticipated.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Three games. The right building combinations go a long way to success. But just as important is getting the dice to roll the right numbers/colors for the buildings that you need to activate. They dice may be working perfectly for someone else and not for you, or vice versa. I don’t want to plan dozens of cubes each round for future rounds, but I mostly don’t want my results to be entirely determined by getting lucky based on the buildings I happen to have acquired. Still, you can do better when you pick a plan and follow through with it from the beginning of the game.

I have already made a fix that goes a long way to helping: you can pay 1 GC to make a die +/- 1 (this affects only you). This helps A LOT, but it's still not quite enough.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Macao's wind rose mechanism is a flash of minor brilliance. You ideally want as many action cubes as possible from the dice you pick, but getting more cubes also means that you'll be waiting longer to access them. Equally important is the combination of cubes you get, because a good part of the game rests on activating cards to be used in a rudimentary engine. 

Despite the game being largely dependent on dice rolls, there are ways to manage luck. You have to compete for turn order to avoid getting stuck with dross from the draft, powerful cards are balanced out by complex activation costs, and so on. Of course, you will occasionally struggle with even simple activation costs, and this brand of randomness won't sit well with many. However, the luck still falls within acceptable limits for me, and I like the feeling of building up for big turns where I'm able to fire off several cards at once.

Of course, Macao still has a few real shortcomings. On the surface level, the pointless abbreviations (GC, AC, BP) are a minor annoyance. Digging deeper, this game can yield to some nasty AP when observing your opponents' potential moves, especially in claiming city quarters. And even if you aren't AP-prone, a few rounds can really grind by as you manage a bunch of accumulated cubes while your opponents simply watch on. The game also scales differently as competition for shipping and claiming city quarters tightens up considerably with more players, so pick your poison.

All things considered, Macao is a great medium-weight game of luck management with a sleek ruleset. Achieving a good spread of action cubes provides most of the game's tension. The VP paths here are remarkably constrained, so I consider this the most accessible of Feld's big box efforts. Having long given up on Feld's attempts to adhere to theme, I can even overlook the fact that goods are able to teleport onto ships in the middle of the ocean.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
Fans of drafting cards with a bunch of different ways to convert cubes will love this game.  Six cube currencies combine in different ways to buy stuff.  You can't save them from turn to turn so you never know if you will be able to match currencies together so you play the odds.  At that point the game is yours to lose rather than to win.  If the odds don't line up (either from random card flips of what you can buy, or by getting the cubes you need from the dice) your screwed sucka!  I don't mind random screwage in eurogames like Peloponnes because the game is only 1/2 an hour long here if you get screwed you have to sit out the rest of the game.

The end game is really dull, too.  Many of these engine building euros suffer from each player's ability to do too much and the turns drag, but here it is the opposite, as you reach closer to the end of the game there are fewer options for you (the cubes start drying up).  

The real madness is the VP cash-in, who knows if what you are buying into is worth it.  If you are unable to generate the money when the market is right you are screwed.  When three gold will get you 10vps that is the equivalent of delivering all the goods to one region.  How can you feel good about continuing to play the game when you miss that?

The theme not only is nonexistent, but is aberrant from any reality.  Macau has such a cool geographical lay out, yet they ignored it to the point where they put the ocean to the west of the city.  WTF!

The dice wheel is cute though, it might be nice to see this in a war game for unit resupply.

RATING: 9
Wow, this [b]is[/b] a great use of dice! There is randomness in the initial turn order, but the rest of the randomness/luck in the game is shared equally amongst players since everyone uses the same dice roll from which to choose 2 specific dice (and all have access to the same cards, based on turn order). You have to try to plan up to 6 turns in advance, arranging to get the cubes you want (hoping, of course, that the right dice rolls come up). Cubes (6 colors) are used to activate cards (which generally give you a bonus each turn), collect goods from the city, move your ship between various ports demanding those goods, advance on the turn order track, etc. But because you cannot count on a specific die color rolling a specific number (even after 6 tries), you have to maintain flexibility and find other back-up ways of using any cubes you have been collecting ... so choose the right cards and collect the right goods tokens!

As someone commented, this is like Yspahan crossed with Duck Dealer: great use of dice, with accumulation of action cubes used to do stuff. Unlike Duck Dealer, though, there is a reasonable limit on how many cubes you can collect and the actions are much simpler -- meaning that there will be no 30-minute turns!

The main innovation here is the dice-rolling and cube-distribution-rondel-thingie. The gold-for-points mechanism is also interesting. Cards-that-do-things is an oldie but a goodie. All in all, this is a terrific cube-pushing resource-managing theme-deficient (although I never missed it -- the underlying mechanisms are really quite interesting and fun) Euro I am looking forward to playing more!

But I'm not sure why they couldn't say "gold" instead of "GC", and "cube" instead of "AC", or just use icons!!

RATING: 8
This is the second of Stefan Feld's games I've played after In the Year of the Dragon.  ItYotD is the better game in my opinion but I still quite liked Macao, there's an awful lot more depth to mine out of it.  It is one of the heavier resource-allocation Euros I've played and games like this typically put me off before I even give them a chance, they always just seem so dry and stuffy.

I was interested in this one though because of, and one of the things I still loved after playing it, the wind rose component.  The wind rose is a wheel that you turn one tick every round and the pile of resources it points to after turning is your supply for that round, those piles are accumulated by rolling different colored dice each round and getting the number of cubes of that color in that many rounds for use.  This is a really unique and interesting twist on typical resource management.  You have to plan in the short as well as long term to collect all the different colored cubes necessary for what you want to do because that supply is only available for that round.  You also take a point penalty if ever you don't have cubes available for a round.  This game forces you to think on a rolling 6-round time table.

There is also a good variety of things to do with those resources every turn.  There are buildings/people/offices to activate then use once activated, gold to acquire and then use to purchase victory points (or use certain activated cards), city quarters to buy, a boat to navigate, goods to sell at port, and a turn order marker to move.  The amount of depth and strategy necessary to find the most profitable combination of these actions and plan for them on such a long time table provides a very attractive amount of depth and variability for me and the random factor of the dice makes it not feel as mechanic as other resource management games.

This also makes it so, like ItYotD, there are many paths to victory which also gives this game a lot of replay value since you can try many different strategies and they could all be viable.  My first game was with 3 players and each player played a little differently and our final point totals were very close.  2 players vied for first position on the wall to ensure they got their first choice of building but one focused on card combos that allowed him to collect and sell goods at port while the other focused on collecting large amounts of money to buy the point tribute every turn and cards that would give him an every turn point bonus.  Meanwhile I neglected my position in the turn order and focused instead not on card combos but simply buying a large connected city string and selling as many of those goods as I could at port.  And my opponents attacked an opportunity in the final round by buying up the two cards I could have played with my last turn resources so I took an end-of-game point loss for not completing my entire tableau so there is opportunity to interact though that is one of the shallower areas of the game (player interaction).

RATING: 7
Another comment compared the game to riding a bucking bronco, which I think captures the feeling pretty well. In the first few turns you're forced to take cards you can't afford, then have to try and work them into a plan for the (uncertain) future.

There's an annoying luck-factor, though it can be mitigated and I don't think a player with a balanced strategy should suffer too many negative effects from it. But lucky rolls or lucky draws [i]can[/i] have a huge positive effect.

What I most like is the forced forward-planning. You can make a reasonable showing in Puerto Rico playing purely tactically, but Macao literally [i]cannot[/i] be played without planning several turns in advance. It's an interesting experience.

--

I have to lower the rating to reflect my general distaste for, in particular, keeping track of upcoming characters. In practice, though, this game was just fired by Trajan and Tzolk'in. Everything I liked about Macao, they do better. Traded for Imperial.

RATING: 10

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
17-Mar-10: Played Macao last week. Innovative cube-collection mechanic. But too much luck in cards available per round. Still, the card powers are nice. 

It's interesting with how you collect cubes. There's 6 different types of cubes in 6 different colours, right? Each round, someone rolls 6 dice. Each dice corresponds to the colour of a type of cube, so a red dice gives red cubes. Then everyone decides which 2 dice to choose; they are allowed to pick the same dice as another person so player A and player B can both choose red and blue dice. Depending on the number on the dice, you get cubes. So a 5 on red dice produces 5 cubes. But now the catch - you've got a little wheel in front of you which depicts 7 segments:

http://www.boardgamegeek.com/image/668203/macao

When you pick a 5 red die and get 5 cubes, these cubes go next to the Segment number 5. After everyone gets their cubes, everyone turns their wheel clockwise 1 segment. Notice the segment with the arrow? That segment now points to the cubes that you get to use this round. So those 5 red cubes you just collected? You'll only get to use them 5 rounds from now. 

So you need to forward plan a bit. This, I like.

You also must try to spend all your cubes because unspent cubes this round are wasted. This, I don't mind.

What I don't like is that you don't know what cards are going to turn up 5 rounds from now. To me, it seems to be counter-intuitive to the whole "forward planning" thing. 

A big part of the game is setting yourself up in a future Killer Round with heaps and heaps of cubes to spend. The cards break the rules; card powers are like "Get an extra red cube when you collect red cubes", or "Game End: Get 7 VPs", or "Everytime you pay Gold to get VPs, get an extra 2 VPs". There's quite a bit going on with the ships on the board, and the markers in the city, but that is fairly straight forward.

07-Apr-10: First choice is key in Macao IMHO

02-Nov-10: There's no such thing as a "quick game of Macao" :-p

RATING: 7
There's quite a lot going on in Macao, and the many options make for difficult choices, given the limited resources available to players. There also typically are difficult decisions to be made regarding whether to take resources in bulk regardless of type or take fewer of a type that may give you the right combination to activate a building. The paths to victory are varied and the the decisions along the way are always tough, which for me makes Macao a winner. One big caveat, however, for potential buyers: the graphic design on the cards is just awful. Instead of a picture of a coin, Alea chose to use the confusing initials GC (for gold coin). Instead of a picture of a cube, you pay and receive AC's (action cubes) and victory points become PP's (prestige points). Just to carry this crazy nomenclature to its extreme, you also acquire B/P cards (Building/Person). Terminology also is confusing. When you pay resources to put a card into play, you do not build it, but rather "activate" it. And while many cards can be activated each round, this is rather called "using" the activated card. The coins also are cheesy; a missed opportunity by Alea to enhance the theme a bit. So ... some terrible graphic and terminology decisions by a company that, as a veteran publisher of true gamer's games, should know better. But ... the stellar game play definitely makes this a keeper despite all the superficial flaws.

RATING: 7
Macao uses a dice-manipulation mechanic in which one player rolls the dice at the beginning of each round. Players use the dice as a menu of action-points to choose from. There are six dice rolled, all different colors. If players choose a dice with a 1 on it, they'll get on action point (APs come in the form of cubes) in that color, and it will be used in the action round immediately following. If a player chooses a 2, they get two APs in that color for the following round, and so on, all the way up to choosing a die with 6 pips, and waiting to use 6 APs, but 5 turns later. The trick is taking cards that can be completed by the APs, and that will combo well for your plans. The hard part is knowing exactly how to plan for those since information is limited as players only know for certain of 2 cards that will be up for grabs at the start of each round. What's more is that players [i]must[/i] take cards to start each round, and you do it [i]before[/i] you roll the dice. So there's a bit of a hope-I-get-lucky element in every turn.

My concern going into the game is that there wouldn't be enough competition on the central board. But I was pleasantly surprised that there is. There are basically three things players are competing for on the central board: 

1. The Wall Race
This is strictly advancing your token further along a track. The track determines play order--which can be a big deal with other races on the board, and choosing your cards first each round--and it's also a tie breaker for final scoring.

2. City Locations
Players use APs to grab certain wares tiles in the city of Macao. They place ownership markers of their colors on those city sections, and will score 2x the number of their biggest connected network of ownership tokens.

3. Shipping
Each player has a ship that sails to distant markets overseas. They use the wares they've collected in Macao to deliver. The first player to arrive with wares, gets more points than those who come later.

I like the game. The dice-manipulation is simple and it keeps the game moving along at a decent pace. I honestly thought it would be a longer game than it ended up being, which is nice.

I have Trajan in my collection--another Feld title--and I think that with time, I'll feel compelled to choose between them because they share the same "feeling". In both games, you've got a unique action-selection mechanic that drives everybody's mini games going on on the central board. This one is lighter. There are less things going on than in Trajan. I also think the rond-cala in Trajan is a bit more brain burning than the windrose in Macao. I usually prefer heavier experiences over lighter ones. So my guess is Trajan will win the battle in the long haul.

I give Macao a 7 out of 10 after just one play. This is very subject to change with more plays.

RATING: 8
An absolutely fun romp that may turn out to be a good game as well.  I'm so enamored with the clever windrose mechanism that I don't know if the game itself will have much lasting power.  The variety of cards that come out (since so many of them are out of the game each time) means you can't quite plan ahead (not to mention that your opponents may swipe the card you want just because it's cheap).  8 plays in, and I'm still enjoying this very much.

Update: The next 6 plays saw me getting progressively more disappointed with the game.  Not only are you at the whims of the dice, but you never know what goods you will need in the future since you don't know anything about the upcoming cards.  I really wanted to like this game, but the lack of control was too much for me to take.  

As a last ditch effort, we tried to tweak the game a bit to give us more control.  We overdid it the first time, making offices too powerful.  After taking a look at some of the variants suggested in the forums we gave it another try and the game was great fun again.  We'll have to hammer out the details on which of the variants we prefer, but it looks like Macao is back to stay!
