RATING: 5.5
First Impression:

A long eurogame attempt with very interesting mechanics and a somewhat working theme.

I loved the rondel (dice excluded) and first player selection and I liked the tribute system. The game flows well. Minimum downtime. Great scope and passable theme (which is an exception for Feld)

To Mr. Feld's credit, this is the best implementation of dice I've seen in a euro. It almost gives me hope that one day there will be a euro with proper use of dice. Not this one though. The game would really be better without them. As it is now, the combination of dice+cards makes for a very chaotic game. Skill plays also a significant role, but it's like playing piano in a brothel - it helps being mozart, but other "sounds" will probably dominate anyway.

All in all, I liked the concept and flow of this game, but I was highly dissapointed by the artificial chaos that the dictated use of dice creates.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
The game revolves around rolling six dice.  You pick up the number of resources corresponding to the number of the die, but you do not have access to those resources for the corresponding number of turns.  

The game then presents with ways to spend your resources which is highly skewed towards "getting there first."  This creates a delicate balancing act of generating more resources to be less effective later, or less resources to be more effective now.  With the randomness of the dice and card draw, its impossible to play perfectly.

Throw in the subgame of deciding whether to focus on shipping one or two types of goods, building a long chain of housing districts, being spread out in the resources you collect, hone in on a specific color, focusing on creating gold generating combos, drafting cards based upon the strength of the reward, focusing on easy to fulfill cards (etc. you get the point) while dancing through a sea of unknown and randomness.  

The game forces you to have a flexible plan, which makes it a beautiful thing.  The game has so many decision points, but combined with so much randomness with the dice rolls and the blind card draws.  It would seem the game is determine by luck, until you see the same person winning time after time.  It has a relationship between the dice and cards which was counter intuitive to me at first.    

This game is unlike any game I have played before.  It is incredibly rich and varied with so much to explore.  I can see how other gamers on this site are off put by the lack to perfectly execute their long term plan, but I can not recommend this game enough.  Probably not worth buying if you think you will only get to play this once or twice, but if not, there is a lot of game to investigate with repeated plays.

I think the "fun" is infused with the sense of gambling, without a sense of press your luck.  Namely, the two gambles on the GC to PP conversion, and selecting cubes based upon fulfilling missions early.  The gamble depends strongly on position in the game, contingency plans, and you ability to acquire a solid contingency plan.  This feel of gambling also gives the game a sense of something special.  Only four plays, so too soon for a ten, but damn I love this game.

RATING: 6
SOLD (8/25/11) --- This is the kind of game that I particularly feel incapable of evaluating after a single play. However, I do know I want to play it again, which is a good sign. I tried Macao at BGG.CON (thanks to Dale Yu and his teaching prowess), and have been looking forward to an opportunity to give it another shot ever since. The one thing I do think for certain is that, while Macao is very different from Feld's two preceding Alea titles - Notre Dame and In the Year of the Dragon - it nonetheless feels very similar. Fans of Notre Dame and/or In the Year of the Dragon are certainly more likely to feel right at home with Macao. On its face Macao appears to be a kinder, gentler game than either of those, but underlying that I believe is a nasty engine that ruthlessly hands out negative points to unwary gamers. The game has prompted many to trot back out the phrase that was used incessantly upon the release of Yspahan a few years back - "it uses dice, but in an interesting/unique/clever way." Macao has the group collectively roll a handful of multi-colored dice, and then everyone individually selects which three dice they'd like to use. You then get action points in the color of the die equal to its face value, but don't receive them until X turns later where X equals the face value of the die. This is a very straightforward in practice because if you choose the red die showing a 2 then you get 2 red action points 2 turns from now, but if you choose the blue die showing a 5 then you get 5 blue action points 5 turns from now. You need these actions points to build buildings (different color action points build different types of buildings) that give you victory points and money (used for purchasing victory points of course). You can also use action points to advance on the turn order track (analogous to the Year of the Dragon turn order track), or to purchase chits representing various goods, or to sail your ship throughout Europe to deliver those chits for victory points. Did you catch that? You sail your ship throughout Europe. It doesn't make any sense. It didn't hit me until a fellow player pointed it out, but once she did I couldn't stop thinking about it. Just look at that board. You sail around to cities like Marseilles, Amsterdam, and Hamburg, and the space between them is all water. Is this some post-apocalyptic Europe where the entire continent has been flooded except for a few select cities? I smell a re-theme! Regardless, as you've probably gathered from the general description above, the theme is irrelevant because this is a game about the cleverness of the interlocking mechanics, in the classic Feld mold. The reason I say up front that I need to play more to properly evaluate Macao is that I can't say after one game how frustrating the die rolls will be and how much they'll ruin your best laid plans. I don't mind dice in Descent, Queen's Gambit, or War of the Ring, but in an Alea game about collecting action points to convert into victory points, dice playing too large a role feels very incongruous. It looked upon first blush as if there were potentially enough decisions in Macao to mitigate the dice, which I hope to confirm through subsequent plays.

Purchased: January 25, 2010

RATING: 8
A really clever use for dice in a game. Everyone has access to the same ones, but not everyone will want the same or even get what they wanted. This is just how the game plays. Many interesting choices that combine set collection, pickup and deliver, actions allowed by the amount of cubes (that you get through the dice) you have in every round.

Macao is a hard game to play - it is from a time when Feld didn't give you points for almost everything and actually liked to take away points! It is tough to control your cubes for actions, while having to build things, activate cards, collect goods, move your ship, fight for turn order, be ready to pay for points when the value is good. All this from the same pool of cubes. I loved it! But I definitely can see why someone could see is a chore!

For what it is worth, I think it is great, though awesomely dry on theme.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
I was drawn to this despite a lot of reasons not to like it: Cards with special powers, lots of randomness, somewhat solitairish play, point-saladishness, weak theme, micromoves (in the boat movement), and the negative opinion of prominent geekbuddies.

I think it's pretty good though. Fun at least. I will list some factors that save it:
1. It plays quickly, at least until you have a ton to work with. You typically can't do very much on your turn especially early in the game.
2. Interaction is visible in multiple places: Wall position determines your card drafting and turn order. There's sort of an area control game with placing markers. When you have goods of a type there is a race to be the first to deliver it.
3. I also very much like that the dice outcome each turn applies to all players equally. If everyone were rolling the dice separately the randomness would be intolerable.
4. While the special powers on the cards are annoying, they aren't very complicated, and the cards also play a larger and more interesting role as liabilities that you need to "pay off" and get off your board so that it doesn't fill up and earn you punishment markers. This kind of reminded me of In the Year of the Dragon.

Uncertainty about whether the role of luck is too great (particularly in what cards come up and are available for you to draft on your turn) keeps my rating down a little bit. There is some intriguing promise that maybe through sufficient and clever planning I can make the stars align for me, but that may not be true.

RATING: 8
Un puerto con distritos y mercancias, una empalizada y barcos que llevan las merancias a las islas. 12 turnos. Se escogen cartas por orden de la empalizada, se tiran dados de seis colores, se escogen dos dados cada uno y se ponen los cubos correpondientes en el numero correspondiente de la rosa de los vientos. Durante la accion puedes activar las cartas, comprar mercancia en distritos, transportarla en barcos a las islas o avanzar puestos en la empalizada, y al final cambiar monedas por puntos de prstigio. Te penalizan con PP por no tener provision de cubos en el turno siguiente, sexta carta en el tablero y cartas no activadas al final de la partida. Muy dinamico, tablero bonito y duracion justa

RATING: 6
I really like the dice and wind-rose mechanic. Plays very well with two, am interested to see how it does with more.

Edit: Yes, yes and yes! Macao is easily my favorite of Feld's games. Tactical play with dice, planning ahead, and a great dash of luck and variety. While the correct combination of cards can make or break you, it never feels like you're doing poorly due to sub-optimal play.

While Burgen von Burgund has a similar feel, I vastly prefer this game due to it's larger planning element. Normally I fancy myself a tactician rather than a strategist, but when it comes to Macao, I get out my binoculars and scan the horizon with glee!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I haven't felt this let down by a game in a long time. Perhaps I came into it with expectations that were too high-- my eternal fandom for 'In the Year of the Dragon' made any Stefan Feld an auto-buy for me. But, much like the tedium of the four hour bore-fest called Loyang, Macao is extraordinarily procedural.

My favorite feeling in a game is the tension from having way to much to do and not enough time to do it; I love that perfect and by-design torture of being pulled in twenty directions... wanting, no needing(!) to do it all. Right. Now.

Part of the frustration I felt with Macao is that for the first two to three rounds you do feel that gamey-goodness. Macao teases you, lulls you into thinking that you are about to play something great. But the longer the game progresses-- most oppressively in the last third of the game-- the more the choices are dictated by capricious dice rolls that collide (for better or worse) into an engine that is already too built to change.

A game like Macao offends by constraining your choices, squeezing them down narrow pathways until your work as a gamer is to simply eliminate untenable options and pick from the leftovers. While the compass-rose-dice-wheel-thing is fresh, it is also dictatorial: your choices are so narrowly determined by the fickle hand of fate that later, once you have access to the action cubes, the allocation feels limited and prescribed. 

In effect what this means is that the game comes down to drafting cards. Yes, you want to get some synergy. But the choices in each round of card drafting are also narrow. If you are pursuing a particular path because of what you have built already, the card drafting too is often obvious. This leaves the "game" in allocating dice rolls for action cubes. But here again the choices are constrained. You have to fill out all the empty spots or get dinged with negative points. And when you are free of that threat, you are left with this:
"One gray, two black are useless-- although four gray would have been great, to bad that isn't what was rolled-- I guess that leaves 4 green or 3 red... yawn."

Macao let me down not because it is a bad game, but because it gives the illusion-- but not the substance-- of a thinky, tense game. You want to build this fabulous engine and tweak the motor for maximum effect, but because the course you set for yourself often clashes with the confining and fixed possibilities of the dice, all that is left is for the monkey to pull the levers and for the engine to run itself.

RATING: 6
Euro that has some inventiveness behind its mechanics.  Feld's common struggle-against-the-game style is here, in the form of the punishment tokens that you take if you don't meet minimum thresholds for actions or building.  Theme is still pretty light.  We happen to be traders, this time traders from China.  The victory points feel just as arbitrary as usual, with bonus buildings and persons that you can acquire with the right combination of cubes.  As with most Euros, there's very little story to be had, beyond "I grabbed this slightly more potent card combination and was able to get more VPs than my opponents as a result."

The goods roll is definitely the star of this show.  Cards and punishment tokens will force taking some suboptimal rolls, and it's up to the players to decide when to sacrifice a bit to get what they need now, rather than later.  There's some juicy decisions here.  I wish the game would be a little longer and more involved, with structured building patterns like, say, [gameid=35677] to allow slightly more predictable paths to victory.  As it is, this is a very tactical game.

RATING: 6
Macao presents a unique twist on the dice management concept. In Macao, 6 dice are rolled every turn (1 for each color resource). You get to take resources in two colors of your choice; the number on the die represents both how many resources you get and how many turns you'll have to wait for them. It is a tension of less now versus more later. The ultimate twist is that you can never store resources, so you must coordinate to get the color combinations you need. 

The juxtaposition of planning and randomness will be jarring to some people, but I've found the challenge an intriguing one. I think once you understand the dice odds and temper your expectations accordingly, as well as learn to work backwards from what you already have in hand rather than plan forwards to depend on particular dice rolls, then the game unveils itself as an enjoyable mid-weight experience. 

Finally, what I like about the game as well are the myriad of person and building cards. There are 120 or so cards included, but only 40 or so are used each game. They went with an Agricola variety approach so to speak, and it really adds to the experience. 

I'm usually down on games that are this self-absorbed/non-interactive, but Macao presents such a unique and interesting puzzle that I'm not too bothered by it.

RATING: 8
Before Macao, I didn't understand the infatuation everyone had with Stefan Feld. I didn't see the point of games that arbitrarily give you points for every action you take. My first game of Macao completely changed my perspective. The unique way the dice are used to generate resources and create a time frame for them to be delivered on a later turn was logistical puzzle I had never encountered before. It's fascinating trying to decide between instant gratification and investment for bigger rewards, all the while needing to sync up those shipments, large or small, so that they can be fully utilized.

Nothing in the game is even remotely thematically grounded. You are generating resources by collecting dice, to pay for cards and buy points. But the puzzle those resource dice create is so wonderfully challenging. Once I learned to love the logistical aspects of Macao, it blew the doors off my disinterest in point salad Euros. Now I'm grabbing Feld games as fast as I can find them and diving into their unique puzzles. I'm glad I get it now, because they really are some of the most satisfying games in my collection.

RATING: 7
My first 2-player game was rather disappointing. It's not a bad game, it's just that there's nothing about it that's particularly interesting. The tribute track shouldn't be in this game, it feels completely out of place. 

The windrose is very intriguing, but it's a weird mixture of tactics and strategy - you make those elaborate future plans, but you can still be screwed by dice on round-to-round basis and there's nothing you can do about it really. 

There are some cool card combos that may occur, but the way the cards come out is totally random and you can't really make long term plans regarding your engine. You don't see enough cards in a 2-player game. There's also not enough competition for wares. This is clearly a euro designed for 3-4 players. I will give it another go with higher player count - if it doesn't click with me, I'll sell it without regrets.

[b]/update:[/b] Bumped from 6 to 7. I played it again with three players. After a few plays the thought process behind every decision became more enjoyable for me. When you acknowledge the fact that you have to manage the risk and take more tactical rather than strategic approach to the game, the puzzle becomes more intriguing and less frustrating. The positives started to outshine the negatives, planning for those big turns can be really satisfying, but Macao as a whole is still far from being as good as Trajan or Bora Bora.

RATING: N/A
In the Far East we are Portuguese traders trying to set up our shops in the quarters of the city to pick up goods tiles like tea, china, and jade.  We select and build office cards, influence local politics, and later in the game will ship our goods to markets back home in Europe.  Each player has a windrose that represents planning for future actions.  Different color dice and cubes represent different systems of the city.  One of the first games I can think of that forces you to commit to future plans.  Too dry for me and frankly too hard to balance the short term resource needs and committing to future turn plans which almost always go awry.

RATING: 7
My initial plays left me a little uninspired, similar to Notre Dame - the sort of game I would always agree to play but not suggest or push to play. This is mainly because it is just too heavy for most occasions and runs too long, especially with 4 and as people get bogged down with their big turns late in the game. I can imagine with the right group who know the game there is some really great strategy and variation with the cards. However, I don't see myself able to unlock this value.
'17/12/03: I have concern about how the card draw is swingy - for example, you may have a card that synergizes with another, but that other card may never come up. I'm not sure how to mitigate this - if it comes up you're in luck, if not, you can do something else. The final few card draws are also quite swingy because you may or may not have the cubes to build them. You can mitigate this by piling up cubes on the last turn. It is an interesting game, just not a classic that I will return to.

RATING: 7
Macao is a strange one and seems to have a conflicting design at its core.  It punishes you for not planning ahead, yet it discourages you from hoarding resources.  Combine that with the random nature of the dice roll and card draw, you can never be sure whether the colors you kept for future rounds will actually be of use.  I like the different take on the action point allocation, as well as the multiple paths to victory through the city quarter, goods delivery and prestige purchase.  Your strategy will be mostly dictated by the cards you acquire.

There are a lot of good ideas in Macao, but I think the disparity between the different elements of the game ultimately hurts it, as well as adding a lot of analysis into one's consideration that will more often than not prove to be useless.  I enjoy it but I don't love it, and I doubt it will ever become a favorite.

Update #1: After two more plays I've warmed up to Macao a bit.  I've decided to embrace the randomness rather than to fight against it, as a result, I actually found it to be a necessary charm to the game.  Yes, it is frustrating not to be able to plan to far ahead, but that is what risk management is all about.  It also make each experience different enough to warrant another look.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.2
This is a Feld-game, what means that you’ll get a game with lots of possible choices and a pasted on theme. If you like Feld-games, you’ll also love this game. If you hate them, you’ll hate this one.

As we like Feld a lot, this was another one to get. Biggest minor point of this game, is also the biggest plus for this game. The windrose…. Very nicely done with the dice, and the way you can use this. You’ll have to decide if you want to do a long- or a short-term planning. Pain in the ass, as it comes to turning the windrose to the next round. This is were it should have come with an extra board for storage of the cubes and the possibility to mount the windrose on the board with a plastic screw.

Not too difficult to teach to other players. Enough depth to stay interesting to advanced players. Just another very nice game (or maybe we should call it great) of Stefan Feld.

RATING: 7.5
Ooh, brilliant logistical, speculative, gambling agony!

Macao is a great one for the masochist in all of us.  Its core mechanism is a heptagon for each player, depicting the six values on a die, and an arrow.  There are six colors of [i]action cubes[/i], and the corresponding six dice in those same colors.  Each of the twelve turns, the dice are rolled, and each player may take cubes according to two dice of her choice.  If I choose the 5 that rolled on the blue die and the 2 that rolled on the red die, I take 5 blue, and put them outside the 5 position of my heptagon, and put 2 red outside the 2 position of my heptagon.  Next, the heptagon rotates clockwise, and the action cubes at which it points are my very ephemeral supply for this turn.  Those are the only ones I can use this turn, and this is the only turn I can use them — leftovers get discarded.  The other thing that happens each round, and before the dice are rolled, is a forced draft of one card per player.  Those cards have costs of 1-4 cubes in 1-4 colors.  Of course, our dear Stefan likes to motivate the players through punishment, so you are punished every time you let those cards accumulate too much on your board (i.e. without "activating" — buying — them).  There are two other types of punishment, as well.  The good part is that the cards you activate are yours to use for the rest of the game, and they enable you to do special things.

Macao is a game that encourages repeated plays to try out different methodologies, and because there are dice involved (as well as opponents, of course), it may take several tries just to execute one methodology successfully.  In the first four days after opening it, we'd already played it three times.  It would have been four, but we were too sleepy the third day from starting it late at night the first two....

One "issue" this has in common with [thing=31594][/thing] is that you [-]basically must[/-] almost need to vie for start player.  [-]You can't[/-] It can be tricky to win off the table scraps.  Knowing that, however, play and have fun!

Another problem is that, due to the tactical nature of the individual turns, there's a little more downtime than I would prefer; however, it's not too bad.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
